The Five Possible Stances on Bodies
Imam Razi enumerates five possible stances on bodies and elaborates the first three.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five logically possible stances on whether bodies are eternal or originated, and the details of the first three stances, including the views of religious groups and philosophers.

Core Idea
Only five logical possibilities exist for the origin of bodies—each reflects a fundamental theological or philosophical commitment.

Imam Razi begins his analysis of the origin of bodies by mapping out the logical terrain. He shows that reason alone presents only five possible positions. This lesson lays out these five possibilities, then examines the first three in detail, identifying which groups hold each view and how they refine their positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the five stances
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice how Razi summarizes the five possible logical combinations: both essences and attributes originated; both eternal; eternal essences with originated attributes; originated essences with eternal attributes; or suspension of judgment.
This classification is exhaustive—no other logical possibility exists.
2
Examine the first stance
The first stance (both essences and attributes originated) is held by most religious groups: Muslims, Jews, Christians, and Magians. Reflect on why this is the majority view among theists.
This stance affirms creation ex nihilo in both essence and properties.
3
Explore the second stance
The second stance (both eternal) is attributed to Aristotle and his followers. Note the nuance: celestial bodies are eternal in essence and attributes except movements (each preceded by another without beginning); elemental bodies have eternal matter but originated forms and accidents.
Aristotle's view is sophisticated: not all aspects of bodies are eternal equally.
4
Analyze the third stance
The third stance (essences eternal, attributes originated) is held by most pre-Aristotelian philosophers. They further subdivided: some said the primal element is earth, fire, air, or vapor, with other elements derived by rarefaction or condensation.
This shows that even within a single stance, there were diverse theories.
5
Consider the remaining two stances
Note that Razi only mentions the fourth and fifth stances (originated essences with eternal attributes, and suspension) without elaboration. This lesson focuses on the first three that are historically articulated.
The logical completeness of five stances is important even if only three are detailed here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة: في شرح مذاهب الناس في هذه المسألة
المذاهب الممكنة في هذه المسألة لا تزيد على خمسة، لأنه إما أن يقال: الأم جسام محدثة بذواتها وصفاتها أو يقال إنها قديمة بذواتها وصفاتها. أو يقال: إنها قديمة بذواتها محدثة بصفاتها. أو يقال إنها بصفاتها محدثة بذواتها . أو يتوقف في كل واحد من هذه الأقسام. أما الاحتمال الأول : وهو القول بأن هذه الأجسام محدثة بذواتها وصفاتها. فهو قول الأكثرين من أرباب الملل وهم المسلمون واليهود والنصارى، والمجوس. وأما الاحتمال الثاني: وهو القول بأن الأجسام قديمة بذواتها وصفاتها. فهو قول بعض الفلاسفة وتفصيل مذاهبهم : أن الأجسام الفلكية قديمة بذواتها وقديمة بصفاتها المعينة. إلا حركاتها . فإن كل واحدة من حركاتها مسبوقة بحركة أخرى، لا إلى بداية . وأما الأجسام العنصرية. فإن هيولاها ،قديمة وأمَّا صورها وأعراضها فكل واحد منها مسبوق بآخر، لا إلى بداية. وهذا مذهب (أرسطاطاليس) وأتباعه من المتقدمين والمتأخرين. وأما الاحتمال الثالث: وهو أن الأجسام قديمة بذواتها محدثة بصفاتها. فهذا قول أكثر الفلاسفة الذين كانوا قبل (أرسطاطاليس) ثم هؤلاء فريقان : الفريق الأول : الذين قالوا : الذوات القديمة كانت أجساما . وهؤلاء منهم من قال : أصل الأشياء هو التراب. وكون العناصر الثلاثة الباقية عنه بالتلطف. ومنهم من عكس وقال : أصل الأشياء هو النار ، وكون العناصر الثلاثة الباقية عنها بالتكاثف. ومنهم من قال أصل الأشياء هو الهواء. وكون العنصر اللطيف عنه: بالتلطف، وكون العنصرين الكثيفين عنه : بالتكاثف. ومنهم من قال أصل الأشياء هو البخار، وكون العنصرين اللطيفين عنه : بالتلطف، وكون العنصرين الكثيفين عنه : بالتكاثف.
Third Introduction: Explanation of the schools of people on this issue.
The possible schools on this issue do not exceed five. Because either it is said: bodies are originated (muhdatha) in their essences and attributes, or it is said that they are eternal (qadima) in their essences and attributes, or it is said that they are eternal in their essences and originated in their attributes, or it is said that they are originated in their essences and eternal in their attributes, or one suspends judgment on each of these divisions.
As for the first possibility: which is the statement that these bodies are originated in their essences and attributes. It is the statement of the majority of the people of religions, and they are the Muslims, Jews, Christians, and Magians.
As for the second possibility: which is the statement that bodies are eternal in their essences and attributes. It is the statement of some philosophers. And the detail of their schools: that celestial bodies are eternal in their essences and eternal in their specific attributes, except their movements. For each of their movements is preceded by another movement, without beginning. As for elemental bodies, their matter (hayula) is eternal, and as for their forms and accidents, each one of them is preceded by another, without beginning. This is the school of Aristotle and his followers among the earlier and later.
As for the third possibility: which is that bodies are eternal in their essences and originated in their attributes. This is the statement of most of the philosophers who were before Aristotle. Then these are two groups: The first group: those who said that eternal essences were bodies. Among these are those who said: the origin of things is earth, and that the remaining three elements come from it by rarefaction. And among them are those who reversed and said: the origin of things is fire, and the remaining three elements come from it by condensation. And among them are those who said: the origin of things is air, and the subtle element comes from it by rarefaction, and the two dense elements come from it by condensation. And among them are those who said: the origin of things is vapor, and the two subtle elements come from it by rarefaction, and the two dense elements come from it by condensation.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the possible stances on whether bodies are eternal or originated. The author begins by stating that logically there are only five possibilities, which he lists succinctly. He then elaborates each one. The first stance (both essences and attributes originated) is the view of most religious adherents. The second (both eternal) is attributed to some philosophers, specifically Aristotle and his followers, with a nuanced position: celestial bodies are eternal in essence and attributes except movements (which are perpetual but each preceded), while elemental bodies have eternal matter but originated forms and accidents. The third stance (essences eternal, attributes originated) is attributed to most pre-Aristotelian philosophers, with further subdivisions. Some held that the eternal essence is body, and they debated the primal element (earth, fire, air, or vapor) from which others derive through rarefaction or condensation. The passage thus systematically categorizes the range of cosmological theories.

Key Terms
أجسام
محدثة
قديمة
ذات
صفات
فلاسفة
هيولى
صور
أعراض
تلطف
تكاثف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The stance that essences are eternal and attributes originated is the same as both being eternal.
Correction: The second stance holds both eternal, while the third limits eternity to essence only, with attributes being originated.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, how many logically possible stances are there on whether bodies are eternal or originated?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The possible positions on the origin of bodies are limited to five logical combinations, and the first three are historically held by religious groups and philosophers with varying nuances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the five possible stances on the origin of bodies, which one most closely aligns with your own belief? Have you ever critically examined why you hold that view, or have you simply inherited it? What would it take for you to sincerely investigate the alternatives?
?
Imam Razi presents these stances with apparent neutrality. Do you find it easy to honestly consider philosophies that contradict your own, or do you instinctively dismiss them? Reflect on a time you encountered a worldview that challenged your beliefs—how did you respond? What does your reaction reveal about your intellectual humility before the truth?
?
The third stance (eternal essence, originated attributes) was held by pre-Aristotelian philosophers who had deep convictions about the primal element. How much are you willing to question the fundamental assumptions of your own worldview? Are you prepared to follow the evidence wherever it leads, even if it upsets your comfort or community?

First Position: Bodies Created in Essence and Accidents
Al-Razi defines the first position and lists its adherents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first of five possibilities regarding the origin of bodies: that they are created in both essence and attributes, a view held by most religious communities.

Core Idea
Most religions agree bodies are created in essence and attributes.

Before examining arguments, Al-Razi catalogs possible positions on the origin of bodies. The first, and most widely held, is that bodies are originated in every respect—both their essences and their attributes. This view spans Muslims, Jews, Christians, and Magians, forming a broad consensus among religious communities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the concise style: Al-Razi merely states the position and its adherents.
2
Identify the position being described
Focus on the claim: bodies are originated in their essences and attributes. What does 'originated' mean here? It means created ex nihilo, not eternal.
Al-Razi does not yet defend this view, only presents it.
3
Note the communities mentioned
Al-Razi lists Muslims, Jews, Christians, and Magians (Zoroastrians). This shows that belief in bodily creation is common to these religions, contrasting with philosophers who might hold alternative views.
The Magians are often included as a religious community in classical Islamic heresiography.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why would Al-Razi start with this position? It sets up the field for discussion. The majority view is stated first, then he will examine the others.
This classification is part of a broader dialectical method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الاحتمال الأول : وهو القول بأن هذه الأجسام محدثة بذواتها وصفاتها. فهو قول الأكثرين من أرباب الملل وهم المسلمون واليهود والنصارى، والمجوس.
As for the first possibility: it is the statement that these bodies are originated in their essences and attributes. So it is the statement of the majority of the followers of religions, namely the Muslims, the Jews, the Christians, and the Magians.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage defines the first position: the view that bodies are originated (created) both in their essences and their attributes. Al-Razi notes that this is the position of the majority of people of religious communities (arbāb al-milal), specifically mentioning Muslims, Jews, Christians, and Magians (Zoroastrians). He is not yet arguing for it, merely classifying it as one of the five possibilities.

Key Terms
الاحتمال الأول (al-iḥtimāl al-awwal, first possibility)
محدثة (ḥāditha, originated)
ذوات (dhawāt, essences)
صفات (ṣifāt, attributes)
الملل (al-milal, religious communities)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which religious communities does Al-Razi mention as holding the first position?
A) Muslims and Jews only
B) Muslims, Jews, Christians, and Magians
C) Muslims, Christians, and Jews only
D) Philosophers only

Key Takeaway
The first possibility is that bodies are created in both substance and attributes, a view shared by most religious communities including Muslims, Jews, Christians, and Magians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe this first position—that your body and all its qualities are created by Allah. But when you wake up tomorrow, will you genuinely treat your breath, your strength, your thoughts as gifts given anew each moment? What would change if you lived that belief from dawn to dusk?
?
Al-Razi lists Magians alongside Muslims as holding this view. Does it surprise you that non-Muslims can share a truth about creation? Does that challenge you to respect the common ground, or does it make you uncomfortable? Honestly, how do you feel about being in agreement with them on this fundamental matter?
?
Your very existence is defined by this 'first possibility'—you are not self-sustaining. Does that thought fill you with gratitude and humility, or do you habitually act as if you own your life? What specific step can you take today to show that you truly believe your essence and attributes belong to Allah?

The Water-Origin Theory of the World
Razi presents the water-origin cosmogony.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ancient philosophical view that water is the primordial substance and how the world was formed from it through motion and transformation.

Core Idea
Water as the origin — the first substance generates the cosmos through motion.

This lesson introduces one of two ancient cosmogonies presented by Imam Razi. According to this view, water is the original element, and through its own movement, it produces heat, foam, and smoke, from which earth and heaven are formed. Understanding this helps appreciate the kind of philosophical speculation that Islamic theologians like Razi engaged with and refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation, noting the sequence of events from water to earth and heaven.
Pay attention to the causal chain: movement → heat → foam & smoke → earth & heaven.
2
Identify the causal chain
Notice how movement causes heat, then foam and smoke lead to earth and heaven. What is the role of heat?
3
Reflect on the refutation
Remember that Razi will later refute this view. Think about why a theist might reject this mechanistic, eternalist explanation.
This view denies a Creator and posits eternal matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : أصل الأشياء هو الماء. ثم إن الماء تحرك، فأوجبت حركته سخونة، فتصاعد على وجه الماء زبد وارتفع منه دخان فتكونت الأرض من ذلك الزبد والسماء من ذلك الدخان. ومنهم من قال : أصل العالم أجزاء جسمانية كرية صلبة. ولما كانت أجزاء الخلاء متشابهة، لم يكن بقاء كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء في حيز معين من ذلك أولى من حصوله في حيز آخر، فلا جرم وجب فيها أن تكون متحركة من الأزل إلى الأبد. ثم اتفق لتلك الأجزاء أن تصادمت على شكل خاص، فتمانعت بسبب حركاتها المتدافعة فتكونت السماوات بهذا الطريق ثم إنها لما استدارت وكان باطنها مملوءًا من الأجسام عرض لما كان في غاية القرب من السموات، أن تسخن جدا وهو النار ـ وعرض لما كان في غاية البعد من السموات، أن تكاثف وبرد جدا - وهو الأرض - والذي كان قريبا من النار، هو الهواء. والذي كان أبعد منه الماء. لأن الهواء ألطف وأسخن من الماء، ثم اختلطت هذه العناصر الأربعة، بسبب حركات الأجرام الفلكية، وتولدت المركبات من المعادن والنبات والحيوان.
And among them is he who said: The origin of things is water. Then water moved, so its movement necessitated heat, so foam rose on the surface of the water and smoke ascended from it, and the earth was formed from that foam and the heaven from that smoke. And among them is he who said: The origin of the world is spherical solid corporeal parts. And since the parts of void were similar, the persistence of each one of those parts in a specific location was not more preferable than its occurrence in another location, so necessarily they were bound to be in motion from eternity to eternity. Then it happened that those parts collided in a specific shape, so they resisted each other due to their conflicting motions, and the heavens were formed in this way. Then when they rotated and their interior was filled with bodies, what was extremely close to the heavens became very hot—that is fire—and what was extremely far from the heavens became dense and very cold—that is earth—and what was near fire is air, and what was farther than it is water, because air is subtler and hotter than water. Then these four elements mixed due to the motions of the celestial bodies, and compounds—minerals, plants, and animals—were generated.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage classifies two ancient philosophical opinions about the origin of the universe. The first opinion is the water-origin theory, attributed to Thales or similar: water as the primordial substance, which by movement generates heat, then foam and smoke, from which earth and heaven arise. The second opinion is an atomistic theory: the world originates from spherical solid parts (atoms) that are identical in essence and located in a void. Since no part has a privileged location, they are in perpetual motion from eternity; by chance collisions they form the heavens, and from the heavens' rotation, the four elements (fire, air, water, earth) are produced, then compounds. Razi presents these views as part of the third introduction's survey of false doctrines, setting the stage for the upcoming kalam proofs of temporal origination.

Key Terms
أصل الأشياء
الماء
الزبد
الدخان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first opinion presented, from what was the heaven formed?
A) Water
B) Foam
C) Smoke
D) Heat

Key Takeaway
In this ancient view, water is the original element; motion and heat transform it into the earth and sky.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the complexity and order of the universe, do you find it easier to attribute it to chance natural processes (like this water theory) or to a wise Creator? Be honest: which explanation feels more comfortable to your mind, and why? This week, when you see something beautiful in nature, pause and consciously remind yourself that it is the work of Allah, not an accident.
?
The passage presents a purely mechanical explanation for the origin of the world. Is there any area of your life where you unknowingly adopt a similar mindset—explaining away events as mere coincidence or natural cause, without acknowledging Allah's will? Identify one specific event from the past week and try to see it as part of Allah's plan. Write it down and reflect on how that changes your reaction.
?
If you were to reject the water-origin theory and affirm that Allah created the heavens and the earth, what practical difference would that make in how you live today? Would it affect your sense of purpose, your gratitude, or your trust in Allah's wisdom? Before this day ends, act on one of these: pray a prayer of gratitude for creation, or help someone see the Creator through nature.

The Atomistic Theory of the World
Razi presents the atomistic cosmogony with spherical parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the atomistic cosmogony that explains the universe as arising from eternal motion and collision of solid spherical particles in a void.

Core Idea
Atoms in motion — random collisions create the heavens and earth.

This lesson covers the second of two ancient cosmogonies presented by Razi. This atomistic view posits indestructible spherical parts (atoms) that move eternally due to the indifference of the void. Their chance collisions form the heavens, and the rotation of the heavens generates the four elements and all compounds. This theory is a precursor to later atomism and is refuted by kalam proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the atomistic description
Focus on the properties of the parts (corporeal, spherical, solid) and the void (similar parts). Understand why they must be in eternal motion.
The key premise: since the void is uniform, no atom has a privileged location.
2
Trace the formation process
Follow the sequence: eternal motion → chance collisions → formation of heavens → rotation → sorting of elements → mixing into compounds.
This is a fully deterministic, mechanical process.
3
Consider the theological implications
How does this theory conflict with the Islamic belief in a Creator and temporal creation? Why would Razi present it only to refute it?
The theory denies creation ex nihilo and purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : أصل الأشياء هو الماء. ثم إن الماء تحرك، فأوجبت حركته سخونة، فتصاعد على وجه الماء زبد وارتفع منه دخان فتكونت الأرض من ذلك الزبد والسماء من ذلك الدخان. ومنهم من قال : أصل العالم أجزاء جسمانية كرية صلبة. ولما كانت أجزاء الخلاء متشابهة، لم يكن بقاء كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء في حيز معين من ذلك أولى من حصوله في حيز آخر، فلا جرم وجب فيها أن تكون متحركة من الأزل إلى الأبد. ثم اتفق لتلك الأجزاء أن تصادمت على شكل خاص، فتمانعت بسبب حركاتها المتدافعة فتكونت السماوات بهذا الطريق ثم إنها لما استدارت وكان باطنها مملوءًا من الأجسام عرض لما كان في غاية القرب من السموات، أن تسخن جدا وهو النار ـ وعرض لما كان في غاية البعد من السموات، أن تكاثف وبرد جدا - وهو الأرض - والذي كان قريبا من النار، هو الهواء. والذي كان أبعد منه الماء. لأن الهواء ألطف وأسخن من الماء، ثم اختلطت هذه العناصر الأربعة، بسبب حركات الأجرام الفلكية، وتولدت المركبات من المعادن والنبات والحيوان.
And among them is he who said: The origin of things is water. Then water moved, so its movement necessitated heat, so foam rose on the surface of the water and smoke ascended from it, and the earth was formed from that foam and the heaven from that smoke. And among them is he who said: The origin of the world is spherical solid corporeal parts. And since the parts of void were similar, the persistence of each one of those parts in a specific location was not more preferable than its occurrence in another location, so necessarily they were bound to be in motion from eternity to eternity. Then it happened that those parts collided in a specific shape, so they resisted each other due to their conflicting motions, and the heavens were formed in this way. Then when they rotated and their interior was filled with bodies, what was extremely close to the heavens became very hot—that is fire—and what was extremely far from the heavens became dense and very cold—that is earth—and what was near fire is air, and what was farther than it is water, because air is subtler and hotter than water. Then these four elements mixed due to the motions of the celestial bodies, and compounds—minerals, plants, and animals—were generated.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage classifies two ancient philosophical opinions about the origin of the universe. The first opinion is the water-origin theory, attributed to Thales or similar: water as the primordial substance, which by movement generates heat, then foam and smoke, from which earth and heaven arise. The second opinion is an atomistic theory: the world originates from spherical solid parts (atoms) that are identical in essence and located in a void. Checkpoint
What is the reason given in the passage for the eternal motion of the atoms?
A) They are created to move
B) The void uniformity makes no location preferable
C) They are alive
D) The heavens push them

Key Takeaway
This atomistic theory explains the universe as eternal, with random atomic collisions generating a rotating heaven that sorts matter into elements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the universe is merely atoms in eternal motion with no Creator, what ultimate purpose does your life have? Do you find that your heart truly accepts this, or does it yearn for a Designer?
?
The passage attributes the formation of the heavens and earth to random collisions. Think of a specific moment of order or blessing in your life—can you honestly attribute it to chance, or do you see Allah's hand in it?
?
This theory denies a beginning. When you wake up each morning, does the thought that the world could be eternal bring you peace or anxiety? How does the Qur'anic declaration that 'He created the heavens and the earth in truth' (al-Ankabut 44) anchor your soul?

Three Sects Denying Bodies as Primal Entities
Razi enumerates three groups who claimed the primal entities were not bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three non-Islamic philosophical positions that claimed the eternal essences were not bodies, and how each proposed an alternative primal entity.

Core Idea
Three ancient schools claimed bodies are not primal: matter-and-form, light-and-darkness, and indivisible units.

In this passage, Razi continues his survey of erroneous views about the nature of eternal entities. He identifies three specific sects that, while denying that bodies are eternal, posited other entities as primal. Understanding these positions helps frame the Islamic theological stance on the temporal origination of the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the clear enumeration of three sects.
The passage begins by labeling this as 'the second group' from a larger classification.
2
Identify each sect's core claim
For each of the three sects, identify what they claim is the eternal primal entity and how bodies arise from it.
The first sect uses Greek hylomorphic concepts; the second is dualist; the third is an atomic unit theory.
3
Observe the logical structure
Notice how Razi presents each sect's view in order, with a brief explanation of their mechanism for body formation.
The third sect is described in more detail, showing the stepwise generation from unit to point to line to body.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why Razi includes these views. They are not Islamic; he is setting up the background before presenting his own proof for the temporal nature of bodies.
These are positions to be refuted, not endorsed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفريق الثاني : الذين قالوا : الذوات القديمة ما كانت أجسامًا. فهؤلاء ثلاث طوائف. الطائفة الأولى : الذين قالوا : الأجسام مركبة من الهيولى والصورة. فالهيولى كانت قديمة، وكانت خالية عن الجسم، ثم حدثت الصورة الجسمية فيها، فحدثت الأجسام. والطائفة الثانية : الذين قالوا : العالم إنما تولد من امتزاج النور بالظلمة، وأما الأنوار والظلمات، فإنها قديمة. وهذا قول الثنوية. والطائفة الثالثة : الذين قالوا : أصل الأجسام الوحدات. وذلك لأن الوحدة إذا كانت مجردة عن الوضع والإشارة كانت مجرد وحدات . وإذا كانت الوحدة مشارًا إليها صارت نقطة. فإذا تركتب نقطتان صار خطا . وإذا تركب خطان، صار سطحًا. وإذا تركب سطحان صار جسما، فأصل الأجسام الوحدات وهي أمور قديمة قائمة بذواتها.
And the second group: those who said that the eternal essences were not bodies. These are three sects. The first sect: those who said that bodies are composed of matter (hayula) and form. Matter was eternal and devoid of body, then the corporeal form occurred in it, so bodies came to be. The second sect: those who said that the world came to be only from the mixture of light and darkness; as for lights and darknesses, they are eternal. This is the saying of the dualists (thunawiyya). The third sect: those who said that the origin of bodies is units (wahadat). This is because when the unit is abstracted from position and indication, it is mere units; and when the unit is pointed to, it becomes a point. Then when two points combine, they become a line; when two lines combine, they become a surface; when two surfaces combine, they become a body; so the origin of bodies is the units, and they are eternal substances subsisting by themselves.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents the second major group of philosophers who denied that bodies are eternal, but instead posited other eternal entities. Razi divides them into three sects. The first is the hylomorphic view (Greek-inspired): eternal matter (hayula) receives form, producing bodies. The second is dualism (Manichaean or similar): light and darkness are both eternal, and their mixture generates the world. The third is an atomistic view based on units (points): indivisible units, when concatenated, produce lines, surfaces, and bodies; these units are eternal. Razi is cataloguing these erroneous positions as part of the 'third introduction', before presenting his own proof for the temporal origination of bodies.

Key Terms
الهيولى والصورة
النور والظلمة
الثنوية
الوحدات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These three sects are considered Islamic theological schools.
Correction: These are pre-Islamic or non-Islamic philosophical positions (Greek, Manichaean, and possibly atomistic) that Razi is enumerating as erroneous, not Islamic sects.

Checkpoint
According to the third sect, what is the immediate origin of a line?
A) A unit
B) Two points
C) A surface
D) A body

Key Takeaway
Razi catalogues three ancient accounts of eternal non-corporeal origins to set up his own proof for the temporal origination of bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered that your own understanding of the world's origin might be shaped by pre-Islamic philosophies? Which of these three sects' assumptions have you unknowingly entertained? How can you purify your belief from such traces?
?
The third sect builds bodies from abstract units becoming points. Do you see how a seemingly innocent assumption (that the universe can be explained by rational reflection alone) leads to a denial of the Creator's free will? What would you say to someone who claims they can deduce the world's origin through pure reason?
?
These sects all posited eternal entities besides Allah. Is there anything in your daily life that you treat as 'eternal' or self-sufficient apart from Allah—like your career, health, or relationships? What would it mean to truly acknowledge that only Allah is eternal and everything else is created?

Two more rejected possibilities: Eternal attributes and suspension
Razi completes the five possibilities with two rejected options.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi considers the fourth and fifth possibilities regarding the world's eternity as untenable, and how he dismisses them.

Core Idea
Razi dismisses the fourth view as self-evidently false and mentions Galen's suspension as merely historical.

Having listed three possibilities about the world's eternity, Imam Razi now completes the enumeration with the fourth and fifth. The fourth view—that the world is eternal in attributes but originated in essence—is rejected as patently absurd. The fifth view of suspending judgment, attributed to Galen, is noted but not adopted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style: each possibility is stated and then dismissed.
2
Understand the fourth possibility
The fourth possibility claims the world is eternal in its attributes but originated in its essence. Razi says this is self-evidently false. Ask yourself why that might be: can attributes exist without an essence?
Attributes are dependent on essence; if essence is temporal, attributes must be too.
3
Understand the fifth possibility (suspension)
The fifth possibility is suspending judgment entirely, attributed to the physician Galen. Razi does not refute it here; he simply records it as a historical position.
This is not presented as a viable option for a Muslim theologian.
4
Reflect on why these are included
Consider why Razi includes a position that no one holds (fourth) and one from a non-Muslim (fifth). This shows thorough enumeration and engagement with all conceivable views.
The aim is to leave no logical possibility unexamined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاحتمال الرابع: بأن يقال : العالم قديم الصفات محدث الذات. وهذا معلوم البطلان بالبداهة فلا جرم لم يقبل به قائل. الاحتمال الخامس : التوقف في هذه الأقسام وعدم القطع بواحد منها. وهو قول (جالينوس).
The fourth possibility: that it be said: the world is eternal in attributes, temporally originated in essence. And this is known to be false by self-evidence, so no speaker has accepted it. The fifth possibility: suspension (tawaqquf) concerning these divisions and not being decisive about any one of them. And it is the saying of Galen.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage completes the enumeration of five possibilities regarding the world's eternity. The fourth is the position that the world is eternal in its attributes (such as form or qualities) but originated in its essence (its substance). Razi deems this self-evidently false, so no one has actually held it—it is a purely logical leftover. The fifth is agnosticism: suspending judgment and not committing to any of the previous possibilities. Razi attributes this to Galen, the famous physician and philosopher. These five possibilities conclude the 'third introduction' of the book, setting the stage for the first proof of temporal origination.

Key Terms

التوقف
جالينوس

Checkpoint

C) It was never proposed
D) It contradicts scripture

Key Takeaway
Razi rejects two further possibilities: the notion of eternal attributes with originated essence is self-evidently false, and suspending judgment (Galen's view) is not a valid theological option.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Galen suspended judgment on a fundamental question. Is there a core belief about God or creation that you have left undecided in your heart? What would it cost you to resolve it, and what might it cost you not to?

Two Interpretations of Place and the Void Proof
Razi presents two interpretations of place and two proofs for the philosophers' view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two competing interpretations of place (hayyiz/makan) in Islamic theology and philosophy, and the philosophers' proofs for place as a dimension.

Core Idea
Place is either a mental construct or a real void dimension — each view shapes our understanding of the universe.

Razi shifts from refuting a doctrine to demanding a clear explanation of 'place' (hayyiz). Theologians and philosophers offer fundamentally different answers: one sees place as a mental fiction, the other as an objective empty dimension. This lesson unpacks both views and the two philosophical arguments for the latter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi sets up the debate by demanding an explanation of place.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Find where Razi presents the theologians' view (place as a mental positing) and the philosophers' view (place as a dimension empty of matter). Note the key difference.
The theologians' interpretation is non-existential; the philosophers' is a real entity.
3
Analyze the first proof
Examine the vessel argument: when water leaves a vessel and no air enters, a dimension remains between the vessel's sides. This dimension is claimed to be place itself.
This argument assumes a void can exist.
4
Analyze the second proof
Consider the stone in flowing water: the stone stays still even though water surfaces change. If place were the surface, the stone should move. Therefore place must be an unchanging dimension.
This argument distinguishes place from the surrounding surface.
5
Synthesize the views
Reflect on why the theologians rejected a void dimension and why philosophers embraced it. How does each view affect what we believe about the universe?
This connects to broader theological debates about space and God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا نترك الاستدلال على فساد هذا المذهب، ونطالبهم بتفسير الحيز، وبتفسير كون الجسم مظروف الحيز. فإن بمجرد المطالبة بهذا التفسير، يصعب الكلام على المستدل.
لا يقال : الحيز له تفسيران :
أحدهما ما اتفق عليه جمهور المتكلمين . وهو أنه ليس أمرًا وجوديًا بل هو أمر يفرضه الذهن، ويقدره العقل، ويحكم بكون الجسم حاصلا فيه.
والثاني : ما ذكره قدماء الفلاسفة وهم القائلون بأن المكان هو البعد، وهو مجرد الطول والعرض والعمق الذي لا يكون حالا في مادة، والجسم عبارة عن الطول والعرض والعمق مع المادة. فإذا حصل الجسم في المكان، فالمعنى أنه نفذ بعد الجسم في ذلك البعد المجرد عن المادة، المسمى بالمكان.
قالوا : والذي يدل على وجود هذا البعد المسمى بالمكان وجوه :
أحدها : إنه لا شك أن بين طرفي الطاس مقدارًا من البعد فالطاس المملوء من الماء إذا قدرنا أن الماء خرج منه ولم يدخل الهواء فيه، فمع هذا الفرض لا بد وأن يبقى بين طرفي الطاس مقدار من البعد والامتداد وذلك البعد قد فرضناه خاليا عن الأجسام. فثبت: أن المكان هو البعد.
وثانيها : أنا لو قدرنا حجرًا واقفا في ماء جار، فالشيء الساكن لا معنى لسكونه، إلا أنه بقي في مكانه أكثر من زمان واحد ولا يمكن أن يكون المكان مفسرا بالسطح المحيط ؛ لأن الماء إذا كان جاريًا على ذلك الحجر لم يبق ذلك الحجر ملاقيا لسطح واحد أزمنة كثيرة. فلو كان المكان عبارة عن السطح، لوجب أن لا يكون ذلك الحجر واقفًا. ولما دلّت المشاهدة على أنه واقف ساكن، علمنا أنه لا بد وأن يكون المكان أمرًا آخر مغايرا للسطح المحيط وما ذاك إلا ذلك البعد الذي نفذ بعد الجسم الواقف فيه.
Then we abandon arguing for the corruption of this doctrine and demand from them an explanation of place (hayyiz) and an explanation of what it means for a body to be contained in place. For merely by demanding this explanation, the speech becomes difficult for the arguer.
It cannot be said: Place has two interpretations:
One of them is what the majority of theologians (jumhūr al-mutakallimīn) agreed upon: that it is not a positive entity (amr wujūdī), but rather something that the mind (dhihn) posits, the intellect (ʿaql) estimates, and it judges that the body exists in it.
The second: what the ancient philosophers (qudamā' al-falāsifa) mentioned, who say that place (makān) is dimension (buʿd), which is mere length, width, and depth that does not inhere in matter, while body is length, width, and depth together with matter. So when the body exists in place, the meaning is that the body's dimension penetrates that dimension abstracted from matter, called place.
They said: What indicates the existence of this dimension called place are aspects:
First: There is no doubt that between the two sides of a vessel there is a quantity of dimension. If we imagine a vessel filled with water and then the water leaves and no air enters, then upon this assumption there must remain between the two sides of the vessel a quantity of dimension and extension, and that dimension we have assumed to be empty of bodies. Thus it is established that place is dimension.
Second: If we imagine a stone standing still in flowing water, the meaning of its stillness is that it remained in its place for more than one time. But it is impossible for place to be interpreted as the surrounding surface, because if water is flowing over that stone, the stone does not remain in contact with one surface for many times. If place were the surface, then the stone would not be stationary. Since observation shows it is stationary, we know that place must be something else different from the surrounding surface, and that is nothing other than that dimension which the body's dimension penetrates while standing in it.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents two interpretations of 'place' (hayyiz) after a preamble. Razi first says he will stop refuting the previous doctrine and instead demand an explanation of place, which alone makes the argument difficult for the opponent. Then he anticipates an objection: 'It cannot be said that place has two interpretations...' meaning someone might object by offering these interpretations. The first interpretation is from the majority of theologians (mutakallimun): place is not a real entity but a mental construct. The second is from the ancient philosophers: place is a dimension (bu'd) that is empty of matter—pure length, width, and depth. Body, by contrast, is these dimensions plus matter. The body's dimensions penetrate this empty dimension, which is called place. The philosophers then give two proofs for the existence of this empty dimension. First, a vessel filled with water: if the water leaves and no air enters, there is still a dimension (emptiness) between the sides of the vessel. That dimension is place. Second, a stone in flowing water: the stone remains still despite the water flowing around it. If place were the surrounding surface of the water, the stone would move because the water surfaces change. But since it stays still, place must be an unchanging dimension that the stone occupies, distinct from the water's surfaces. Thus the philosophers argue for a void dimension as place.

Key Terms
حيز
مكان
بعد
سطح محيط
طول
عرض
عمق
مادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the philosophers' view of place is the same as the theologians' mental construct.
Correction: The theologians see place as a non-existent mental positing, while the philosophers see it as a real void dimension.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is place?
A) A mental construct
B) The surrounding surface of a body
C) A dimension empty of matter
D) The position of a body in space

Key Takeaway
The ancient philosophers defined place as an empty dimension, distinct from the theologians' mental construct, and supported this with arguments from everyday phenomena.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers argued that place is a real dimension that remains even when water leaves a vessel. What 'voids' have you left in your daily life—spaces of silence, prayer, or reflection—that have become empty of meaning? Are you willing to let God fill them instead of rushing to fill them with noise?
?
In the proof of the stone in flowing water, the stone remains still because its true place is the dimension it penetrates, not the changing surface around it. What in your life—your faith, your values, your identity—remains still when the world around you changes? Or do you let the 'surfaces' of society define your position?
?
The theologians saw place as a mental construct. Have you reduced your relationship with God to a mental exercise—a set of beliefs you 'imagine' but do not live out? What would it mean for you to treat your faith as a real dimension of your life that every other part of you must penetrate and inhabit?

Six Answers to the Problem of Temporal Specification
Razi enumerates six answers by the mutakallimūn to the problem of temporal specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six rational answers the mutakallimūn proposed to explain why God created the world at a specific time rather than at another, and how each answer appeals to a different principle (will, knowledge, wisdom, impossibility of eternal creation, change in possibility, or free choice without a preponderator).

Core Idea
Six different answers, but all try to explain why God chose that moment.

The problem of temporal specification asks: why did God create the world at this particular time and not earlier or later? The mutakallimūn offered six distinct responses. In this lesson, we will explore each of these answers, noting how they rely on different aspects of God's attributes or rational constraints.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of six answers
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how each answer begins with a number (first, second, etc.).
The answers are listed sequentially.
2
Identify the key concept in each answer
For each answer, determine whether it relies on will, knowledge, wisdom, logical impossibility, change in possibility, or free choice. Write them down.
3
Reflect on the common thread
Ask yourself: Do these answers solve the problem of temporal specification? What might the philosophers say?
Consider that all answers try to explain why that specific time was chosen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجاب المتكلمون عنه في وجوه :
أحدها: أنك لمَ لا يجوز أن يقال : العلم إنما حدث في الوقت المعين لأن إرادة الله تعالى تعلقت بإيجاده في ذلك الوقت دون سائر الأوقات وليس لأحد أن يقول : لم تعلقت الإرادة بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت، ولم تتعلق إرادته بإحداثه في سائر الأوقات ؟ وذلك لأن تلك الإرادة بعينها وماهيتها المخصوصة اقتضت التعلق بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت، والماهيات لا تعلل.
ثانيها : أنه تعالى عالم بجميع الجزئيات، فكان علمه القديم متعلقا بأن العالم في أي الأوقات يوجد، وفي أيها لا يوجد والإرادة لا تتعلق بالشيء إلا على وفق العلم، فلهذا السبب تعلقت إرادة الله بإحداث العالم في الوقت الذي علم وقوعه فيه، ولم تتعلق بإحداثه في سائر الأوقات.
وثالثها : لِمَ لا يجوز أن يقال: إن ذلك الوقت اختص بحكمة خفية لأجلها خصص الله تعالى إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت دون سائر الأوقات؟ وإذا كان الاحتمال قائمًا سقطت هذه المطالبة.
ورابعها أن إحداث العالم في الأزل محال لأن الإحداث عبارة عن جعله موجوداً بعد أن كان معدوماً، وذلك يستدعي سبق العدم والأزل عبارة عن نفي المسبوقية بالغير، فكان الجمع بينهما محالا.
وخامسها : أن العالم قبل ذلك الوقت ما كان ممكنا بل كان ممتنعا ثم انقلب ممكنا في ذلك الوقت.
و سادسها : أن القادر المختار يمكنه أن يرجح أحد المقدورين على الآخر من غير مرجح كما أن الهارب من السبع إذا عَنَّ له طريقان متساويان من جميع الوجوه فإنه يختار أحدهما على الآخر من غير مرجح والعطشان إذا خير بين قدحين متساويين فإنه يختار أحدهما على الآخر من غير مرجح.
قالت الفلاسفة : لو تأملتم حق التأمل في الحجة التي ذكرتموها لما أوردتم هذه الأجوبة، وذلك لأن حاصل هذه الأجوبة يرجع إلى حرف واحد وهو أن كل ما لا بد منه في إيجاد العالم ما كان حاصلا في الأزل.
وأما الجواب الأول : فلأنكم قلتم بأن إرادة الله تعالى تعلقت بإيجاد العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، فإذن العالم إنما لم يوجد في الأزل لأن حصول ذلك الوقت المعين شرط، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، ففات الإيجاد لفوات شرطه.
وأما الجواب الثاني: فهو أن الوقت الذي علم الله تعالى أن العالم يوجد فيه شرط لوجود العالم، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاضراً، والعالم لا يوجد لأن شرطه ما كان حاضرًا في الأزل.
وأما الجواب الثالث فهو أن شرط دخول العالم في الوجود حضور الوقت المشتمل على تلك المصلحة، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاضرًا في الأزل فالعالم لم يحصل لأن شرط حصوله كان فائنا.
وأما الجواب الرابع : فهو أن انقضاء الأزل شرط الإيجاد، وهذا الشرط ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، ففات الإيجاد لفوات شرطه.
وأما الجواب الخامس فهو أن حضور الوقت الذي حدث فيه إمكان العالم شرط الإيجاد، وهذا الشرط ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، ففات الإيجاد لقوات شرطه.
وأما الجواب السادس وهو أن القادر يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر لا المرجح فمعناه أن دخول العالم في الوجود يتوقف على ذلك الترجيح، ثم إن ذلك الترجيح ما كان حاصلا في الأزل ففات الإيجاد لفوات شرطه.
The mutakallimūn answered it (the objection) in [several] ways: First: Why is it not permissible to say that knowledge occurred only at the determined time because the will of God Most High was attached to bringing it into existence at that time to the exclusion of other times? And no one may say: Why was the will attached to bringing about the world at that time, and not attached to bringing it about at other times? That is because that very will and its specific quiddity necessitated attachment to bringing about the world at that time, and quiddities are not caused. Second: He Most High is knower of all particulars, so His eternal knowledge was attached to which times the world exists and which it does not, and will is not attached to anything except in accordance with knowledge. For this reason, the will of God was attached to bringing about the world at the time He knew it would occur, and not attached to bringing it about at other times. Third: Why is it not permissible to say that that time was distinguished by a hidden wisdom for which God Most High specified the bringing about of the world at that time to the exclusion of other times? And if the possibility stands, this demand falls away. Fourth: That bringing about the world in eternity is impossible because bringing about is an expression of making it existent after it had been non-existent, and that requires the precedence of non-existence, while eternity is an expression of the negation of being preceded by another. So combining them is impossible. Fifth: That the world before that time was not possible but rather impossible, then it became possible at that time. Sixth: That the free agent (al-qādir al-mukhtār) can give preponderance to one of the two power-possibles over the other without a preponderator, just as the one fleeing from a beast, if two equal roads present themselves from all aspects, he chooses one over the other without a preponderator; and the thirsty person, if given a choice between two equal cups, chooses one over the other without a preponderator. The philosophers said: If you had truly reflected on the proof you mentioned, you would not have presented these answers. That is because the conclusion of these answers reduces to a single point: that everything indispensable for bringing the world into existence was not present in eternity. As for the first answer: You said that the will of God Most High was attached to bringing the world into existence at that determined time, and that time was not present in eternity. So the world did not exist in eternity because the occurrence of that determined time is a condition, and that time was not present in eternity. Thus the bringing about was missed due to the absence of its condition. As for the second answer: The time that God Most High knows the world will exist in is a condition for the existence of the world, and that time was not present, so the world does not exist because its condition was not present in eternity. As for the third answer: The condition for the entry of the world into existence is the presence of the time containing that benefit, and that time was not present in eternity, so the world did not obtain because the condition for its obtaining was absent. As for the fourth answer: The passing of eternity is a condition for bringing about, and this condition was not present in eternity, so the bringing about was missed due to the absence of its condition. As for the fifth answer: The presence of the time in which the possibility of the world occurred is a condition for bringing about, and this condition was not present in eternity, so the bringing about was missed due to the absence of its condition. As for the sixth answer—that the powerful one gives preponderance to one of his power-possibles over another without a preponderator—its meaning is that the entry of the world into existence depends on that preponderance, and then that preponderance was not present in eternity, so the bringing about was missed due to the absence of its condition.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents six answers given by the mutakallimūn to the objection that the great proof (the problem of temporal specification) is not sufficient, and then the philosophers' critique that all these answers share a common flaw. The first answer appeals to God's specific will: His will attached to creating the world at a particular time, and since quiddities are not caused (i.e., the will's specific nature explains its attachment), no further cause is needed. The second answer appeals to God's eternal knowledge: He knows when the world will exist, and will follows knowledge, so the will attaches accordingly. The third answer invokes a hidden wisdom: the time was chosen due to a benefit (maṣlaḥah) known only to God. The fourth answer argues that creation in eternity is logically impossible because 'bringing about' (iḥdāth) requires prior non-existence, while eternity (azal) negates any priority. The fifth answer claims that before the chosen time, the world was impossible (mumtaniʿ) and then became possible (mumkin) at that time. The sixth answer asserts that a free agent can arbitrarily choose between two equal possibilities without a preponderator, giving examples of a fleeing person or a thirsty person choosing between equal options. The philosophers then respond by pointing out that all six answers reduce to a single flaw: each assumes that something necessary for the world's existence—the specific time, the presence of that time, the benefit, the passing of eternity, the moment of possibility, or the act of preponderance—was absent in eternity. In other words, each answer implicitly admits that a condition (sharṭ) for creation was not present in the eternal state, which is exactly the point the philosophers are making: that the world's creation at a specific time is explained by the absence of that condition in eternity. Thus, the philosophers argue that the mutakallimūn have not truly avoided the problem but merely relocated it.

الإرادة (al-irādah)
العلم (al-ʿilm)
الحكمة (al-ḥikmah)
الإحداث (al-iḥdāth)
العدم (al-ʿadam)
الأزل (al-azal)
القادر المختار (al-qādir al-mukhtār)
الترجيح بلا مرجح (tarjīḥ bi-lā murajjih)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the six answers are independent refutations of the philosophers' position, each standing on its own.
Correction: The philosophers later show that all six answers share a common flaw: they implicitly admit that a necessary condition for creation was missing in eternity.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, what necessitates the will's attachment to that specific time?
A) God's knowledge
B) The quiddity of the will itself
C) A hidden wisdom
D) Logical impossibility

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimūn offered varied arguments—from divine will to logical impossibility—to explain the timing of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life, do you sometimes question why certain events happen at a particular time, or why prayers are answered at a specific moment? How does the idea that God's will and knowledge are behind that timing change your perspective? Are you willing to trust His wisdom even when you cannot see the 'hidden wisdom'?
?
The mutakallimūn argued that God's will is not caused by anything external. How does this confront your own desire to control or understand every aspect of your life? Are you able to surrender to God's choices even when they seem arbitrary to you?
?
The sixth answer uses the example of a person choosing between two equal roads. In your spiritual journey, have you ever faced two equally valid options and felt the need to choose? How does this example affirm that you can trust your choice made in reliance on God, without needing a clear 'reason'?

The Philosophers' Critique: A Single Flaw in All Answers
Razi presents the philosophers' critique that all six answers reduce to a single flaw.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers critique all six answers by showing they each reduce to the same flaw: that a necessary condition for creation was absent in eternity.

Core Idea
All six answers confess the same flaw: a condition was missing in eternity.

After presenting the six answers, Razi now reports the philosophers' counter-argument. The philosophers claim that each answer implicitly admits that something required for the world's existence—the specific time, the wisdom, the passing of eternity, etc.—was not present in the eternal state. Thus, the answers do not solve the problem but merely restate it in different terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' critique
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Pay attention to how the philosophers reduce each answer to a single point.
Notice the repetitive structure: 'as for the first answer... it is a condition that was absent in eternity.'
2
Trace the reduction for each answer
For each of the six answers, see how the philosophers rephrase it as: 'X is a condition, and X was not present in eternity.' Note the pattern.
3
Evaluate the critique
Decide whether the philosophers' reduction is fair. Does it show a genuine flaw in the mutakallimūn's arguments?
This critique is a key moment in the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجاب المتكلمون عنه في وجوه :
أحدها: أنك لمَ لا يجوز أن يقال : العلم إنما حدث في الوقت المعين لأن إرادة الله تعالى تعلقت بإيجاده في ذلك الوقت دون سائر الأوقات وليس لأحد أن يقول : لم تعلقت الإرادة بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت، ولم تتعلق إرادته بإحداثه في سائر الأوقات ؟ وذلك لأن تلك الإرادة بعينها وماهيتها المخصوصة اقتضت التعلق بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت، والماهيات لا تعلل.
ثانيها : أنه تعالى عالم بجميع الجزئيات، فكان علمه القديم متعلقا بأن العالم في أي الأوقات يوجد، وفي أيها لا يوجد والإرادة لا تتعلق بالشيء إلا على وفق العلم، فلهذا السبب تعلقت إرادة الله بإحداث العالم في الوقت الذي علم وقوعه فيه، ولم تتعلق بإحداثه في سائر الأوقات.
وثالثها : لِمَ لا يجوز أن يقال: إن ذلك الوقت اختص بحكمة خفية لأجلها خصص الله تعالى إحداث العالم بذلك الوقت دون سائر الأوقات؟ وإذا كان الاحتمال قائمًا سقطت هذه المطالبة.
ورابعها أن إحداث العالم في الأزل محال لأن الإحداث عبارة عن جعله موجوداً بعد أن كان معدوماً، وذلك يستدعي سبق العدم والأزل عبارة عن نفي المسبوقية بالغير، فكان الجمع بينهما محالا.
وخامسها : أن العالم قبل ذلك الوقت ما كان ممكنا بل كان ممتنعا ثم انقلب ممكنا في ذلك الوقت.
و سادسها : أن القادر المختار يمكنه أن يرجح أحد المقدورين على الآخر من غير مرجح كما أن الهارب من السبع إذا عَنَّ له طريقان متساويان من جميع الوجوه فإنه يختار أحدهما على الآخر من غير مرجح والعطشان إذا خير بين قدحين متساويين فإنه يختار أحدهما على الآخر من غير مرجح.
قالت الفلاسفة : لو تأملتم حق التأمل في الحجة التي ذكرتموها لما أوردتم هذه الأجوبة، وذلك لأن حاصل هذه الأجوبة يرجع إلى حرف واحد وهو أن كل ما لا بد منه في إيجاد العالم ما كان حاصلا في الأزل.
وأما الجواب الأول : فلأنكم قلتم بأن إرادة الله تعالى تعلقت بإيجاد العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، فإذن العالم إنما لم يوجد في الأزل لأن حصول ذلك الوقت المعين شرط، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، ففات الإيجاد لفوات شرطه.
وأما الجواب الثاني: فهو أن الوقت الذي علم الله تعالى أن العالم يوجد فيه شرط لوجود العالم، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاضراً، والعالم لا يوجد لأن شرطه ما كان حاضرًا في الأزل.
وأما الجواب الثالث فهو أن شرط دخول العالم في الوجود حضور الوقت المشتمل على تلك المصلحة، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاضرًا في الأزل فالعالم لم يحصل لأن شرط حصوله كان فائنا.
وأما الجواب الرابع : فهو أن انقضاء الأزل شرط الإيجاد، وهذا الشرط ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، ففات الإيجاد لفوات شرطه.
وأما الجواب الخامس فهو أن حضور الوقت الذي حدث فيه إمكان العالم شرط الإيجاد، وهذا الشرط ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، ففات الإيجاد لقوات شرطه.
وأما الجواب السادس وهو أن القادر يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر لا المرجح فمعناه أن دخول العالم في الوجود يتوقف على ذلك الترجيح، ثم إن ذلك الترجيح ما كان حاصلا في الأزل ففات الإيجاد لفوات شرطه.
The mutakallimūn answered it (the objection) in [several] ways: First: Why is it not permissible to say that knowledge occurred only at the determined time because the will of God Most High was attached to bringing it into existence at that time to the exclusion of other times? And no one may say: Why was the will attached to bringing about the world at that time, and not attached to bringing it about at other times? That is because that very will and its specific quiddity necessitated attachment to bringing about the world at that time, and quiddities are not caused. Second: He Most High is knower of all particulars, so His eternal knowledge was attached to which times the world exists and which it does not, and will is not attached to anything except in accordance with knowledge. For this reason, the will of God was attached to bringing about the world at the time He knew it would occur, and not attached to bringing it about at other times. Third: Why is it not permissible to say that that time was distinguished by a hidden wisdom for which God Most High specified the bringing about of the world at that time to the exclusion of other times? And if the possibility stands, this demand falls away. Fourth: That bringing about the world in eternity is impossible because bringing about is an expression of making it existent after it had been non-existent, and that requires the precedence of non-existence, while eternity is an expression of the negation of being preceded by another. So combining them is impossible. Fifth: That the world before that time was not possible but rather impossible, then it became possible at that time. Sixth: That the free agent (al-qādir al-mukhtār) can give preponderance to one of the two power-possibles over the other without a preponderator, just as the one fleeing from a beast, if two equal roads present themselves from all aspects, he chooses one over the other without a preponderator; and the thirsty person, if given a choice between two equal cups, chooses one over the other without a preponderator. The philosophers said: If you had truly reflected on the proof you mentioned, you would not have presented these answers. That is because the conclusion of these answers reduces to a single point: that everything indispensable for bringing the world into existence was not present in eternity. As for the first answer: You said that the will of God Most High was attached to bringing the world into existence at that determined time, and that time was not present in eternity. So the world did not exist in eternity because the occurrence of that determined time is a condition, and that time was not present in eternity. Thus the bringing about was missed due to the absence of its condition. As for the second answer: The time that God Most High knows the world will exist in is a condition for the existence of the world, and that time was not present, so the world does not exist because its condition was not present in eternity. As for the third answer: The condition for the entry of the world into existence is the presence of the time containing that benefit, and that time was not present in eternity, so the world did not obtain because the condition for its obtaining was absent. As for the fourth answer: The passing of eternity is a condition for bringing about, and this condition was not present in eternity, so the bringing about was missed due to the absence of its condition. As for the fifth answer: The presence of the time in which the possibility of the world occurred is a condition for bringing about, and this condition was not present in eternity, so the bringing about was missed due to the absence of its condition. As for the sixth answer—that the powerful one gives preponderance to one of his power-possibles over another without a preponderator—its meaning is that the entry of the world into existence depends on that preponderance, and then that preponderance was not present in eternity, so the bringing about was missed due to the absence of its condition.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents six answers given by the mutakallimūn to the objection that the great proof (the problem of temporal specification) is not sufficient, and then the philosophers' critique that all these answers share a common flaw. The first answer appeals to God's specific will: His will attached to creating the world at a particular time, and since quiddities are not caused (i.e., the will's specific nature explains its attachment), no further cause is needed. The second answer appeals to God's eternal knowledge: He knows when the world will exist, and will follows knowledge, so the will attaches accordingly. The third answer invokes a hidden wisdom: the time was chosen due to a benefit (maṣlaḥah) known only to God. The fourth answer argues that creation in eternity is logically impossible because 'bringing about' (iḥdāth) requires prior non-existence, while eternity (azal) negates any priority. The fifth answer claims that before the chosen time, the world was impossible (mumtaniʿ) and then became possible (mumkin) at that time. The sixth answer asserts that a free agent can arbitrarily choose between two equal possibilities without a preponderator, giving examples of a fleeing person or a thirsty person choosing between equal options. The philosophers then respond by pointing out that all six answers reduce to a single flaw: each assumes that something necessary for the world's existence—the specific time, the presence of that time, the benefit, the passing of eternity, the moment of possibility, or the act of preponderance—was absent in eternity. In other words, each answer implicitly admits that a condition (sharṭ) for creation was not present in the eternal state, which is exactly the point the philosophers are making: that the world's creation at a specific time is explained by the absence of that condition in eternity. Thus, the philosophers argue that the mutakallimūn have not truly avoided the problem but merely relocated it.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers are simply rejecting the answers without argument.
Correction: The philosophers provide a logical reduction, showing that each answer admits a missing condition, thus failing to explain why the world began at that time.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is the common flaw in all six answers?
A) They all deny God's power.
B) Each answer admits that a necessary condition was absent in eternity.
C) They all rely on the concept of preponderance.
D) They all use the term 'eternity' incorrectly.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that all six answers rely on the same implicit admission: that a condition for creation was absent in eternity, thus failing to explain why the world began at that specific time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a delay in your life that you cannot explain, do you naturally search for a condition or reason, or do you rest in the knowledge that God's timing is perfect even when it is hidden from you? This lesson shows how human explanations can reduce to the same flaw: assuming a missing condition. How does this challenge your need to understand every delay?
?
The philosophers argued that all six answers confess the same flaw: a condition was missing in eternity. Think of a time you tried to justify God's timing with a reason. Was that reason ultimately an attempt to fill a gap in your understanding? Are you willing to accept that God's will may be the only explanation needed, without adding conditions?
?
The mutakallimūn offered multiple explanations, but all collapsed into one admission. In your own life, do you rely on multiple justifications for your faith or actions that might all share a hidden flaw? Could it be that your explanations are distractions from a simpler truth: that God's command is sufficient? What would it mean for you to trust Him without requiring a reason?

Classification of Views on the Non-Existent (Ma'dum)
Razi classifies positions on whether the non-existent is a thing based on the existence-essence relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi classifies positions on whether the non-existent is a thing, based on the relation of existence to essence, and how this narrows to the actual dispute about possible non-existents.

Core Idea
The status of the non-existent depends on whether essence and existence are identical or separate.

Razi now applies the earlier metaphysical framework to a classic theological debate: Is the non-existent (maʿdūm) a 'thing'? The answer depends on how one understands the relationship between existence and essence. In this passage, he maps out the possible positions and identifies where the real point of disagreement lies, setting the stage for the Ashʿari stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi first lays out logical implications, then specifies the actual point of dispute.
The passage has two main sections: an initial classification based on existence-essence, and then a narrowing to the possible non-existent.
2
Understand the two-fold classification based on existence and essence
Razi first divides thinkers into two camps: those who say existence is identical to essence, and those who say existence is additional. For each, he draws the consequence for the non-existent.
This classification is based on a metaphysical commitment, not on scriptural arguments.
3
See how the dispute narrows to the possible non-existent
Razi then clarifies that the disagreement is only about the possible non-existent (jāʾiz al-ʿadam), not the impossible non-existent. All agree the impossible is pure non-existence.
This narrowing is crucial to avoid straw-man arguments.
4
Identify the three positions on the possible non-existent
Razi lists three views: (1) Most Muʿtazila: essences and realities in both states, (2) Ashʿaris (aṣḥābunā): pure negation, (3) Abu al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī (a Muʿtazilī): same as Ashʿaris.
The Ashʿari view is that before existence, the possible non-existent has no reality at all—it is simply not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذه المسألة فلنرجع إلى المقصود، فنقول : أما من قال : وجود كل شيء نفس ماهيته لزمه القطع بأنه زال بأنه متى الوجود، فقد زالت الماهية، فالقول بأن المعدوم شيء لا يتصور على مذهب هذا القائل أما من قال : الوجود زائد على الماهية فقد اختلفوا في أنه هل يمكن تقرير الماهية عند زوال صفة الوجود؟ فمن جوز ذلك قال : المعدوم شيء، وعنى به أن الماهية من حيث تكون كتقررة حال ما لا تكون موجودة، ومن لم يجوز ذلك قال : المعدوم ليس بشيء. إذا عرفتم هذا فلنرجع إلى تعيين محل النزاع في هذه المسألة فنقول : المعدوم إما أن يكون واجب العدم ممتنع الوجود، وإما أن يكون جائز الوجود جائز العدم. أما الممتنع فقد اتفقوا على أنه محض وعدم وليس بذات ولا بشيء، وأما المعدوم الذي يجوز عدمه فقد ذهب أصحابنا إلى أنه قبل الوجود نفي محض، وعدم وليس بشيء ولا بذات، وهذا قول أبي الحسين البصري من المعتزلة، وذهب أكثر شيوخ المعتزلة إلى أنها ماهيات وذوات وحقائق حالتي وجودها وعدمها، فهذا هو تلخيص محل النزاع.
And when you understand this issue, let us return to the intended topic. We say: As for one who says: The existence of each thing is its essence, it necessarily follows for him that when existence ceases, the essence ceases. So the statement that the non-existent is a thing is inconceivable in this school. As for one who says existence is additional to essence, they differ on whether the essence can be established [taqarrur] when the attribute of existence ceases. Whoever allows that says: The non-existent is a thing, meaning by that that the essence is, as it were, established in the state of not being existent. Whoever does not allow it says: The non-existent is not a thing. When you know this, let us return to specifying the point of dispute in this issue. We say: The non-existent is either necessarily non-existent, impossible of existence, or possible of existence and possible of non-existence. As for the impossible, they agree that it is pure non-existence, not an entity, not a thing. As for the non-existent that is possible of non-existence, our companions (Ashʿaris) hold that before existence it is pure negation, non-existence, not a thing, not an entity. This is also the view of Abu al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī among the Muʿtazila. Most of the Muʿtazilite masters hold that they are essences, entities, realities in both states of existence and non-existence. This is the summary of the point of dispute.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a classification of views on the non-existent (maʿdūm). First, Razi divides based on the relation of existence to essence: (1) If existence is identical to essence, then when existence goes, essence goes; so the non-existent is nothing. (2) If existence is additional, opinions differ on whether essence can be 'established' (taqarrur) without existence. Some say yes, so non-existent is a thing (the 'established' essence); others say no, so non-existent is not a thing. Next, Razi specifies the actual point of dispute: non-existent either impossible (wājib al-ʿadam) or possible (jāʾiz al-wujūd wa al-ʿadam). All agree the impossible is pure non-existence. For the possible non-existent, the Ashʿaris (our companions) and Abu al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī say it is pure negation, not a thing. Most Muʿtazilites say it is essences, entities, and realities both in existence and non-existence.

Key Terms
معدوم (maʿdūm)
ماهية (māhīya)
وجود (wujūd)
تقرر (taqarrur)
ذات (dhāt)
أصحابنا (aṣḥābunā)
أبو الحسين البصري (Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Ashʿari view denies the reality of essences entirely, even in existence.
Correction: The Ashʿari view only denies that essences have any reality in the state of non-existence. In existence, essences are real because existence is added to them.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the position of most Muʿtazilite masters on the possible non-existent?
A) It is pure non-existence, not a thing.
B) It is an essence, entity, and reality both in existence and non-existence.
C) It is impossible to know.
D) It is a thing only when it exists.

Key Takeaway
The Ashʿari position on the possible non-existent is that before existence it is pure negation, not a thing or entity, whereas the majority of Muʿtazila affirm it as an essence and reality even in non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Ash'ari position is true and your very essence is nothing before God creates it, what does that mean for your pride in your own abilities or plans? Are there areas where you act as if you have some inherent reality independent of Him?
?
The Mu'tazila believe essences are real even in non-existence. Have you ever treated a future hope or fear as if it already has a fixed reality, forgetting that only God brings things into being? What would change if you truly saw the future as pure possibility until He creates it?
?
All agree that the impossible non-existent is pure nothing. Is there something in your life that you know is impossible by divine decree—a regret you cannot undo, or a forbidden desire—that you still give mental reality? Can you, today, affirm it as pure negation and turn fully to what God has made possible for you?
