Razi's Taxonomy of Christian Incarnation Claims
Razi classifies possible Christian claims about divine indwelling or union.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise taxonomy al-Razi uses to classify Christian doctrines of incarnation, distinguishing between claims of indwelling/union, bestowal of power, and honorific title.

Core Idea
Al-Razi reduces Christian incarnation claims to three logically exhaustive categories.

Imam al-Razi recognizes the confusion in Christian discourse about the incarnation. Rather than dismiss it, he constructs a clear, logical classification of all possible positions they could hold. This taxonomy prepares the ground for systematic refutation, showing the intellectual rigor of kalam methodology. In this lesson, we explore his classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice that al-Razi begins by noting confusion, then presents a 'precise classification' (تقسيمًا مضبوطًا).
2
Identify the three main categories
The passage first lists two possibilities: indwelling (الحلول) or union (الاتحاد). Then a second possibility: bestowal of power (إعطاء القدرة). Finally a third: honorific title (التشريف).
3
Examine the subdivisions of the first category
Within indwelling/union, they may relate to God's Essence or an attribute, and to Jesus's spirit or body. This creates multiple sub-options.
4
Understand the logical structure
Notice that the categories are mutually exclusive and exhaustive. The second category denies indwelling/union, and the third denies even bestowal of power. This structure sets up clear refutations.
This taxonomy is typical of kalam method: first classify, then refute each possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن النصارى يذكرون الحلول تارة والاتحاد أخرى، وكلامهم في غاية الخبط، ونحن نذكر تقسيما مضبوطًا فنقول : القوم إما أن يقولوا بالحلول أو بالاتحاد، إما لذات الله تعالى أو لصفة من صفاته إما بالنسبة إلى روح عيسى ال أو بالنسبة إلى بدنه، وإما أن لا يقولوا بشيء من ذلك، بل يقولوا : إنه تعالى أعطاه الله قدرة على خلق الأجسام والحياة، وعلمًا بالمغيبات وإما أن لا يقولوا بذلك أيضًا، بل يقولوا إنه تعالى سماه ابنا على سبيل التشريف كما سمى إبراهيم ال خليلا على سبيل التشريف فهذه الوجوه التي يحتملها كلامهم.
Know that the Christians mention indwelling (al-ḥulūl) sometimes and union (al-ittiḥād) at other times, and their speech is extremely confused. We present a precise classification and say: They either claim indwelling or union, either of the Essence of God Most High or one of His attributes, either with respect to the spirit of Jesus (peace be upon him) or with respect to his body; or they claim none of that, but say that God Most High gave him power to create bodies and life and knowledge of the unseen; or they also do not claim that, but say that God Most High called him a son by way of honor, just as He called Abraham (peace be upon him) a close friend (khalīlan) by way of honor. These are the aspects that their speech admits.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the different Christian doctrines related to the Incarnation. Imam al-Razi begins by noting the confusion in Christian discourse, which oscillates between indwelling (ḥulūl) and union (ittiḥād). He then produces a precise taxonomy of their possible claims. First, they might claim either indwelling or union, and this applies either to God's Essence or to one of His attributes, and either to the spirit of Jesus or to his body. Second, they might not claim indwelling or union at all, but instead assert that God gave Jesus the power to create bodies and life and knowledge of the unseen. Third, they might not even claim that, but simply say that God called Jesus a son as an honorific title, similar to calling Abraham a close friend. Al-Razi states that these are the possible interpretations of their statements, setting the stage for a systematic refutation of each position.

Key Terms
الحلول
الاتحاد
التشريف
عيسى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Razi is merely describing Christian beliefs without critical analysis.
Correction: In fact, Razi is constructing a logical taxonomy for systematic refutation, a standard kalam approach.

Checkpoint
How many main categories of Christian claims does al-Razi enumerate?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's taxonomy classifies Christian incarnation claims into three mutually exclusive categories, each of which he will refute systematically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a belief system that seems confused, do you take the time to classify its claims logically before dismissing them, or do you react emotionally? What would it take for you to adopt Razi's disciplined approach?
?
Razi reduces Christian claims to three categories, but have you ever reduced someone's deeply held beliefs to a neat box to make refutation easier? How might that violate the command to argue with wisdom and beautiful preaching?
?
If you were a Christian reading Razi's taxonomy, would you feel understood or attacked? How can you apply Razi's logical rigor while still loving your opponent as a fellow human being? What is one concrete step you will take this week to better understand someone you disagree with?

Razi's Debate on Miracles and Indwelling
Razi recounts a debate where he refutes Christian reliance on miracles to prove indwelling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses the logical principle that absence of evidence does not entail absence of the thing to refute a Christian argument for God indwelling in Jesus alone.

Core Idea
Absence of proof is not proof of absence — a key logical rule in theological debate.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi reports a debate with a Christian interlocutor. He first secures agreement on a basic logical principle: that the lack of evidence for something does not prove that thing does not exist. Then he turns this principle against the Christian's claim that God's indwelling is limited to Jesus because of his miracles. This passage demonstrates the power of logical consistency in interfaith argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the debate passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the logical principle
Notice the key principle the Christian accepts: 'absence of evidence does not mean absence of the thing.'
3
Follow the application
See how Razi applies this principle to the Christian's case for Jesus, forcing him to doubt that indwelling is limited.
4
Reflect on the strategy
Consider why this argument is effective and how it prevents the Christian from using miracles as exclusive evidence.
This connects to broader kalām methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقد ناظرت بعض النصارى فقلت له : هل تسلم أنه لا يلزم من عدم الدليل عدم المدلول؟ فقال نعم قلت له ما الذي يدل على أن ذات الله لم تحل في بدن زيد، وفي بدن عمرو، ولم تحل في بدن هذه الذبابة وهذه النملة ؟ فقال : هذا ممتنع ؛ لأنا إنما أثبتنا هذا الحلول في حق عيسى ال لأنه ظهر على يده إحياء الموتى وإبراء الأكمه والأبرص، فإذا لم يظهر شيء من هذه الأشياء على يد زيد وعمرو فكيف يمكن إثبات هذا الحلول في حقه؟ فقلت له : إنك سلمت أولا أنه لا يلزم من عدم الدليل عدم المدلول، وإحياء الموتى وإبراء الأكمه والأبرص دل على حصول هذا الحلول عندكم، فلا يلزم من عدم هذا الدليل عدم هذا الحلول، فوجب أن تبقى شاكًا في
And I debated some Christians and said to him: Do you accept that the absence of evidence does not entail the absence of the thing evidenced? He said yes. I said: What indicates that God's essence did not indwell the body of Zayd, or the body of Amr, or the body of this fly or this ant? He said: This is impossible, because we only established this indwelling in the case of Jesus (peace be upon him) because reviving the dead and curing the blind and leprous were manifest through him. Since none of these things appeared through Zayd and Amr, how can this indwelling be established for them? I said to him: You first accepted that the absence of evidence does not entail the absence of the thing evidenced. The reviving of the dead and curing of the blind and leprous indicate, according to you, the occurrence of this indwelling. So the absence of this evidence does not entail the absence of this indwelling. Therefore you must remain in doubt about
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a reported debate (munāẓara) between Imam al-Razi and a Christian interlocutor. Al-Razi first gets the Christian to admit a logical principle: the absence of evidence (dalīl) does not entail the absence of the thing indicated (madlūl). Then he asks what evidence shows that God did not indwell ordinary people, flies, or ants. The Christian answers that indwelling is only proven for Jesus because of his miracles. Al-Razi turns the argument around: the Christian has admitted that lack of evidence does not prove absence. Since the miracles only serve as evidence for indwelling in Jesus, their absence for others does not prove that indwelling did not occur in them. Therefore, the Christian must remain in doubt about whether God indwells all creatures. The passage ends mid-argument on page 117, but the point is clear: the Christian's reasoning is inconsistent.

Key Terms
الدليل
المدلول
المعجزة
المناظرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Razi is endorsing skepticism about indwelling.
Correction: He is refuting the Christian's logic, not doubting God's essence. The argument is about consistency, not about actual belief.

Checkpoint
What logical principle does the Christian accept at the start of the debate?
A) Absence of evidence is evidence of absence.
B) Evidence must be miraculous.
C) Absence of evidence does not entail absence of the thing evidenced.
D) Miracles prove indwelling.

Key Takeaway
The absence of evidence does not prove the absence of the thing; this principle can dismantle arguments that rely on selective evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed that because you didn't see evidence of God's mercy in a trial, it must be absent? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to infer absence from silence? Will you commit to withholding judgment next time?
?
Are you, like the Christian in this debate, applying a different standard of evidence to what you want to believe versus what you reject? Think of one belief you hold strongly. Would you require the same level of proof for its opposite? If not, what does that reveal about your intellectual honesty?
?
The principle 'absence of evidence is not evidence of absence' is easy to say but hard to live. Where in your life are you currently treating lack of evidence as proof—perhaps about someone's sincerity, a loved one's faith, or your own worth? What would it look like to remain in doubt instead of rushing to a comfortable conclusion?

The Logical Trap of Hulul
Dialectical argument exposing absurdity of hulul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses a dialectical argument to expose the logical inconsistency in the Christian doctrine of hulul (indwelling).

Core Idea
Absence of evidence does not equal absence of existence—a logical trap for hulul.

This passage presents a debate between Imam Razi and a Christian. Razi first secures agreement on a basic logical principle: lack of evidence does not prove non-existence. He then turns this principle against the Christian, forcing an absurd conclusion. The argument demonstrates the power of dialectical reasoning in theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical premise
Notice how Razi begins by getting the Christian to agree that absence of evidence does not imply absence of the thing evidenced. This is the key premise.
The phrase 'لا يلزم من عدم الدليل عدم المدلول' is the crux.
2
See the Christian's selective application
The Christian applies hulul only to Jesus based on miracles. Razi asks why not in Zayd, Amr, a fly, or an ant. The Christian rejects because no miracles.
The Christian's argument relies on evidence (miracles) for Jesus and lack of evidence for others.
3
Follow Razi's logical twist
Razi reminds the Christian of the accepted principle: lack of evidence does not prove non-existence. Therefore, the Christian must remain doubtful about hulul in other bodies, which is absurd.
The absurdity shows the doctrine's baseness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقد ناظرت بعض النصارى فقلت له : هل تسلم أنه لا يلزم من عدم الدليل عدم المدلول؟ فقال نعم قلت له ما الذي يدل على أن ذات الله لم تحل في بدن زيد، وفي بدن عمرو، ولم تحل في بدن هذه الذبابة وهذه النملة ؟ فقال : هذا ممتنع ؛ لأنا إنما أثبتنا هذا الحلول في حق عيسى لأنه ظهر على يده إحياء الموتى وإبراء الأكمه والأبرص، فإذا لم يظهر شيء من هذه الأشياء على يد زيد وعمرو فكيف يمكن إثبات هذا الحلول في حقه؟ فقلت له : إنك سلمت أولا أنه لا يلزم من عدم الدليل عدم المدلول، وإحياء الموتى وإبراء الأكمه والأبرص دل على حصول هذا الحلول عندكم، فلا يلزم من عدم هذا الدليل عدم هذا الحلول، فوجب أن تبقى شاكًا في حلول الله في بدن هذه النملة وهذه الذبابة، وكل مذهب أدى إلى مثل هذا القول فهو خسيس جدًا.
I debated some Christians and said to him: Do you accept that absence of evidence does not imply absence of the thing evidenced? He said yes. I said: What indicates that God's essence did not indwell in the body of Zayd and in the body of Amr, and did not indwell in the body of this fly and this ant? He said: This is impossible; for we only affirm this indwelling in Jesus because the revival of the dead and curing of the blind appeared at his hands. Since none of these things appeared at the hands of Zayd and Amr, how can this indwelling be affirmed in their case? So I said to him: You first accepted that absence of evidence does not imply absence of the thing evidenced. The revival of the dead and curing of the blind indicate the occurrence of this indwelling according to you. Therefore, the absence of this evidence does not imply the absence of this indwelling. So you must remain doubtful about the indwelling of God in the body of this fly and this ant. And every doctrine that leads to such a statement is very base.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage contains a dialectical proof demonstrating the absurdity of the Christian position. The Christian admits that lack of evidence does not prove non-existence. Then al-Razi argues that since the Christian has evidence for hulul in Jesus (the miracles), but the same miracles are absent for other bodies, the Christian cannot deny hulul in those bodies because absence of evidence does not prove absence. Thus the Christian must be open to hulul in any body, which is absurd. This shows the baseness of the doctrine.

Key Terms
دليل (dalil)
مدلول (madlul)
حلول (hulul)
شك (shakk)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are necessary evidence for hulul; without them, hulul cannot exist.
Correction: The passage shows that absence of miracles does not prove absence of hulul, because lack of evidence does not imply non-existence.

Checkpoint
What logical principle does al-Razi get the Christian to accept?
A) Absence of evidence is evidence of absence
B) Absence of evidence does not mean absence of the thing
C) Every effect has a cause
D) Miracles prove divinity

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument shows that a selective application of a logical principle can expose a theological doctrine as absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your own life do you selectively apply the principle 'absence of evidence is not evidence of absence'? Are you as quick to doubt your own cherished beliefs as you are to doubt those of others?
?
Razi forces the Christian to face the absurd conclusion of his doctrine. What belief do you hold that, if logically extended, might lead to an absurdity you haven't considered? Are you willing to re-examine it honestly?
?
If you cannot prove that God does not indwell in a fly or an ant, does that possibility trouble you? Why or why not? And what does your answer reveal about your trust in God's transcendence?

Refuting Incarnation by Comparing Miracles
Razi refutes incarnation by reductio from Moses' greater miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's reductio ad absurdum argument against the Christian doctrine of incarnation by comparing the miracles of Moses and Jesus.

Core Idea
A greater miracle does not prove incarnation unless you accept it for all prophets.

Imam Razi presents a crisp logical refutation of the Christian claim that Jesus's miracles prove God's incarnation in him. He draws a comparison with Moses's more wondrous miracle, showing that if the lesser implies incarnation, the greater implies it even more—a conclusion Christians reject. This forces them to abandon their argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the core argument.
Notice the forceful language: 'base' and 'contemptible'.
2
Identify the two miracles
Find the miracles of Jesus (reviving the dead) and Moses (turning a staff into a serpent). Note Razi's claim that Moses' miracle is more wondrous.
This comparison is key to the reductio.
3
Understand the logical structure
Razi argues: if Jesus' miracle indicates incarnation, then Moses' greater miracle indicates incarnation even more. Since Christians do not claim incarnation for Moses, their reasoning is inconsistent.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة
حلول الله في بدن هذه النملة وهذه الذبابة، وكل مذهب أدى إلى مثل هذا القول فهو خسيس جدًا.
وأيضًا كما ظهر إحياء الموتى على يدي عيسى فقد ظهر على يدي موسى لأنه قلب العصا ثعبانا، بل هو أعجب؛ لأن انقلاب الخشبة ثعبانا عظيمًا أعجب من انقلاب الميت حيا، فإن دلت معجزات عيسى على الحلول والاتحاد فبأن تدل معجزات موسى على هذا الحلول - وبالله التوفيق - أولى.
وبالجملة: فمذهب النصارى والحلولية أخس وأذل من أن يلتفت العاقل إليه.
The Ninth Issue: The indwelling of God in the body of this ant and this fly, and every doctrine that leads to such a statement is very base. And also, just as the reviving of the dead appeared at the hands of Jesus, it appeared at the hands of Moses because he turned the staff into a serpent; rather it is more wondrous, because the transformation of wood into a great serpent is more wondrous than the transformation of a dead person into a living one. So if the miracles of Jesus indicate indwelling (ḥulūl) and union (ittiḥād), then it is even more fitting that the miracles of Moses indicate this indwelling — and with Allah is success. In sum: the doctrine of the Christians and the Incarnationists (al-ḥulūliyya) is too base and contemptible for a rational person to pay attention to.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of the Ninth Issue, where Imam Razi refutes the Christian doctrine of incarnation (ḥulūl) and union (ittiḥād). He begins by stating that the notion of God indwelling a lowly ant or fly is base, and any doctrine leading to such a conclusion is contemptible. Then he presents a comparative argument from miracles: Jesus' miracle of reviving the dead is undeniably impressive, but Moses' miracle of turning a staff into a great serpent is even more wondrous. If the lesser miracle (reviving the dead) is taken as evidence for incarnation, then the greater miracle (transforming wood into a serpent) would be even stronger evidence, implying that Moses also would be considered an incarnation of God. Since Christians do not claim this for Moses, their argument collapses. He concludes that the Christian and incarnationist doctrine is too lowly for a rational person to consider.

Key Terms
حلول
اتحاد
حلولية
نصارى
معجزات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi's argument is about which prophet is greater or which miracle is more powerful.
Correction: Razi uses the comparison as a reductio ad absurdum: if Jesus' miracle indicates incarnation, then Moses' greater miracle would indicate it even more, which Christians do not accept. Thus their argument is inconsistent.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Razi's comparison between the miracles of Moses and Jesus?
A) To prove Moses is greater than Jesus
B) To refute the Christian doctrine of incarnation by showing it would apply even more to Moses
C) To argue that miracles do not prove prophethood
D) To affirm the incarnation of God in Moses

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi uses a reductio ad absurdum to refute the Christian doctrine of incarnation by showing that the greater miracle of Moses would logically imply it even more, a conclusion Christians reject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief because of a compelling story or miracle, without checking if it leads to logical contradictions in other cases? How does this lesson challenge you to demand consistency in your own beliefs?
?
Razi calls the incarnation doctrine 'base and contemptible.' Are there any beliefs you hold that, if pushed to their logical conclusion, would also be base or inconsistent? Are you willing to examine them honestly?
?
The passage says this doctrine is too lowly for a rational person to consider. How often do you dismiss uncomfortable truths because they seem 'lowly' or beneath you? What is one belief you have dismissed without proper investigation?

Razi's Classification of Views on God's Will
Razi categorizes all opinions about the attribute of will in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six distinct positions on the meaning of God being willing as categorized by Imam Razi.

Core Idea
Razi's classification maps every possible view on God's will.

This lesson presents Imam Razi's comprehensive classification of the schools' views on God's will. He systematically divides the concept into negative and positive interpretations, then further subdivides the positive into those grounded in essence or in a meaning, and so on. The passage is a clear map of the theological landscape, showing how different groups align with logical possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the logical flow from the first division (negative vs. positive) down to the details.
Razi begins with 'اعلم أن المفهوم...' – pay attention to the structure.
2
Identify the first major division
Razi splits the concept into a negative attribute (salbiyya) or a positive attribute (thubutiyya). Find the example of the negative view from al-Najjar.
The negative view equates will with not being overpowered.
3
Trace the subdivisions under 'positive'
Under positive, there are two branches: grounded in essence (bi-dhatihi) or grounded in a meaning (bi-ma'na). Then under 'meaning', eternal or originated. Follow the branches carefully.
The Mu'tazila and Karramiyya fall under originated will.
4
Notice the empty category
Razi mentions that no one holds the view that the originated will subsists in another essence. Why is this important?
This shows that Razi's classification covers all logical possibilities, even those not adopted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث : في شرح مذاهب الناس في كونه تعالى مريدا اعلم أن المفهوم من كونه تعالى مريدا إما أن يكون صفة سلبية أو ثبوتية، أما القول بأنه أمر سلبي فهذا هو المنقول عن النجار أنه قال : ' معنى كونه تعالى مريدا أنه غير مقهور ولا مغلوب' وأما الذين فسروه بمعنى ثبوتي فذلك المعنى إما أن يكون معللا بذاته أو بمعنى. أما الأول : فهو القول الثاني للنجار، وذلك أنه قال: إنه تعالى مريد لذاته وأما الذين قالوا : المريدية معللة بمعنى فذلك المعنى إما أن يكون قديما قائما بذات الله تعالى، وهذا هو قول أصحابنا، وإما أن يكون محدثًا، وعلى هذا التقدير فهذه الإرادة المحدثة إما أن تكون قائمة بذات الله تعالى - وهو قول الكرامية - أو موجودة لا في محل - وهو قول أبي على وأبي هاشم والقاضي عبد الجبار بن أحمد - وإما أن تكون قائمة بذات غير الله تعالى وما رأيت أحدًا اختار هذا القسم، فهذا تفصيل مذاهب الناس في هذه المسألة، ولما كثر خوض المتقدمين في هذه المباحث لا جرم اكتفينا فيها بالقليل من القول.
The third chapter: on explaining the doctrines of people concerning His being, Most High, willing. Know that what is understood from His being, Most High, willing is either a negative attribute or a positive attribute. As for the statement that it is a negative matter, that is what is transmitted from al-Najjar: he said, 'The meaning of His being, Most High, willing is that He is not overpowered nor vanquished.' And as for those who interpreted it as a positive meaning, that meaning is either grounded in His essence or grounded in a meaning. As for the first: it is the second statement of al-Najjar, namely that he said: He, Most High, is willing by His essence. And as for those who said that 'being willing' is grounded in a meaning, that meaning is either eternal subsisting in the essence of God Most High—and this is the statement of our companions (the Ash'aris)—or it is originated. On this estimation, this originated will is either subsisting in the essence of God Most High—which is the statement of the Karramiyya—or existing not in a locus—which is the statement of Abu Ali, Abu Hashim, and Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar ibn Ahmad—or it is subsisting in an essence other than God Most High, and I have not seen anyone choose this division. So this is the detailed exposition of the doctrines of people in this issue. And since the earlier scholars delved deep into these investigations, we have contented ourselves with a brief discussion.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a classification (tasnif) of all possible views on the meaning of God being 'willing' (murid). Razi begins by stating that the concept can be either a negative attribute (sifa salbiyya) or a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya). Under negative, he cites al-Najjar's view: that it means God is not overpowered or vanquished. Under positive, he divides further: either the meaning is grounded in the essence itself (al-Najjar's second view: God is willing by His essence) or grounded in a meaning (ma'na). This meaning itself is either eternal (the Ash'ari position) or originated. The originated is then subdivided: subsisting in God's essence (Karramiyya), existing not in a locus (Mu'tazila: Abu Ali, Abu Hashim, and Qadi Abdul Jabbar), or subsisting in another essence (no one holds this). Razi notes that he only gives a brief account because earlier scholars have covered it extensively.

Key Terms
صفة سلبية (negative attribute)
صفة ثبوتية (positive attribute)
معلل بذاته (grounded in His essence)
معنى (meaning)
قديم (eternal)
محدث (originated)
قائمة بذات الله (subsisting in God's essence)
لا في محل (not in a locus)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think there are only a few views; Razi enumerates six distinct positions.
Correction: Razi's classification shows that there are exactly six logical possibilities, some of which are historically adopted by different schools.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's classification, which group holds that the will is originated and subsisting in God's essence?
A) Ash'aris
B) Mu'tazila
C) Karramiyya
D) Najjar

Key Takeaway
Razi's classification shows that every possible interpretation of God's will falls into one of six logical categories, with only five actually adopted by theological schools.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider al-Najjar's negative interpretation of God's will—that He is not overpowered—does your heart feel a greater sense of God's sovereignty? In what area of your life do you most need to affirm that God is not overpowered?
?
Razi presents the Ash'ari view that God's will is an eternal meaning subsisting in His essence. Does this challenge your previous understanding of God's will as something that changes? What does it mean for you personally that God's will is eternal and unchanging?
?
If you had to defend either the Karramiyya or Mu'tazili view that God's will is originated, what would be your deepest motive? Is it more important to protect God's transcendence or to affirm His real relationship with time? How does your answer reflect your own relationship with God?

The Four Points of Dispute on Divine Speech
Razi summarizes the four points of dispute with Mu'tazila and states the first station.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four points of disagreement between the Ash'aris and their opponents regarding the divine attribute of speech, and the first station affirming inner speech as distinct from will and belief.

Core Idea
The dispute over God's speech is actually four distinct debates.

Imam Razi condenses the entire controversy about the divine attribute of speech into four precise points. He shows that the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya do not simply deny God's speech; they deny it at multiple levels. This passage sets the stage for the detailed proof of inner speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Notice the structure: Razi lists four conditional points, then mentions the first station.
The passage uses a repetitive pattern: 'yarunkara... wa bi-taqdiri taslimihi...'
2
Identify the four points of dispute
List each of the four denials in order. The first is the denial of a meaning distinct from wills and beliefs. What are the next three?
Each denial builds on the previous one being hypothetically accepted.
3
Understand the context of the first station
Razi says the first station (proving inner speech as distinct) has already been presented. This means the current passage is a summary before moving to subsequent proofs.
The first station is the foundation for the other three points.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة والكرامية ينازعون أصحابنا في كل واحد من هذه المواضع الأربعة، فأولا ينكرون إثبات معنى مغاير للاعتقادات والإرادات، وبتقدير تسليمه ينكرون كونه موصوفًا به وبتقدير تسليمه ينكرون كونه قديمًا، وبتقدير تسليمه ينكرون كونه واحدا، فهذا تلخيص محل النزاع في هذا الباب. أما المقام الأول وهو إثبات أن كلام النفس أمر مغاير للإرادات والاعتقادات، فقد تقدم تقريره على أحسن الوجوه.
The Mu'tazila and the Karramiyya dispute with our scholars in every one of these four matters: first, they deny affirming a meaning distinct from beliefs and wills; and assuming its acceptance, they deny that He is described with it; and assuming its acceptance, they deny that it is eternal; and assuming its acceptance, they deny that it is one. So this is a summary of the point of dispute in this chapter. As for the first station, which is the affirmation that inner speech (kalam al-nafs) is a matter distinct from wills and beliefs, its exposition has already been presented in the best manner.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage introduces the broader dispute over God's attribute of speech. Razi identifies four points of contention with the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya: (1) whether there exists a meaning (ma'na) that is distinct from God's will and beliefs (i.e., inner speech), (2) whether God is described with that meaning, (3) whether it is eternal, and (4) whether it is one. He then states that the first station—proving that inner speech is a meaning distinct from wills and beliefs—has already been established in a previous discussion. This sets up the subsequent arguments.

Razi notes that proponents of created speech argue from transmitted and rational proofs.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Razi organizes opponents' proofs into transmitted and rational categories.

In this passage, Razi sets the stage for discussing the arguments made by those who assert that God's speech is created (hadith al-kalam). He immediately clarifies that their case rests on two distinct types of evidence: transmitted (manqul) and rational (ma'qul). This classification is crucial because it determines how he will respond to each type. The passage then begins to list the transmitted arguments, though the text is cut off, hinting at a detailed enumeration to follow.

The Three Mu'tazili Objections Against Seeing Allah
Al-Razi enumerates the three Mu'tazili objections negating the beatific vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational arguments the Mu'tazila used to deny the beatific vision and how each objection is constructed.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila used three distinct proofs to argue that seeing Allah is impossible.

Al-Razi presents the sum of Mu'tazili arguments against the vision of Allah. Each objection is built on a different premise—facing, impression, and color/shape. Understanding these objections is essential for engaging with the Ash'ari response. Let's examine each one carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three objections
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that the passage explicitly states 'shubhat' (objections) and enumerates them.
The first objection is supported by three sub-points.
2
Understand the first objection (muqabala) and its sub-points
The first objection argues that vision requires facing the object. The sub-points discuss seeing one's face in a mirror and objects in moisture, concluding that only things facing or in the ruling of facing can be seen.
Abu al-Husayn al-Basri claimed necessary knowledge for this.
3
Analyze the second objection (inṭibā‘) and its premise
The second objection states that vision requires the impression of the object's form in the eye. Since Allah has no form, He cannot be seen.
4
Examine the third objection (lawn wa shakl) and its inductive proof
The third objection argues that all visible things have color and shape, proven by induction. Allah transcends these attributes, so He cannot be seen.
This sums up their objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكانت في حكم المقابلة. وثانيها: أنا نرى وجوهنا في المرآة، ويستحيل أن يكون الوجه مقابلا لنفسه، إلا أن الشعاع يخرج من العين إلى المرآة، ثم ينعكس من المرآة إلى الوجه، وبهذا الطريق يكون الوجه جاريا مجرى المقابل لنفسه. وثالثها: الشيء الذي يوضع في الرطوبة فإنه وإن لم يكن في مقابلة العين، إلا أن شعاع العين ينعطف عليه ويصير مرئيا. فهو أيضا في حكم المقابل. إذا عرفت هذا فتقول: إن أبا الحسين البصري ادعى العلم الضروري بأن ما لا يكون مقابلا، ولا في حكم المقابل، يمتنع أن يرى. الشبهة الثالثة وهي شبهة الانطباع - وهي أن كل ما يصير مرئيا، لابد وأن تنطبع صورته ومثاله في العين، والله تعالى لا صورة له ولا مثال، فوجب أن تمتنع رؤيته. الشبهة الرابعة: أن كل ما كان مرئيا، فلا بد له من لون وشكل. ودليله الاستقراء. والله تعالى منزه عن ذلك، فوجب أن لا يرى. فهذا مجموع شبههم في نفي الرؤية.
So it was in the ruling of facing. The second of them: that we see our faces in the mirror, and it is impossible for the face to be facing itself, but rather the ray emerges from the eye to the mirror, then reflects from the mirror to the face, and in this way the face becomes like that which is facing itself. And the third: an object that is placed in moisture, for even if it is not in front of the eye, the ray of the eye bends upon it and it becomes visible. It too is in the ruling of facing. If you know this, then you say: Abu al-Husayn al-Basri claimed necessary knowledge that whatever is not facing nor in the ruling of facing is impossible to be seen. The third objection is the objection of impression — that whatever becomes visible must have its form and likeness impressed in the eye, but Allah Most High has no form or likeness, so it is necessary that His vision be impossible. The fourth objection: that whatever is visible must have color and shape. Its proof is induction. Allah Most High is transcendent above that, so it is necessary that He not be seen. This is the totality of their objections in negating the vision.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage enumerates the three objections (shubah) of the Mu'tazila against the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). The first objection (shubhat al-muqabala) is supported by three sub-points: (1) a face seen in a mirror is effectively facing itself via reflected rays; (2) an object in moisture is seen by bent rays, making it in the ruling of facing; (3) Abu al-Husayn al-Basri claimed necessary knowledge that only what is facing or in its ruling can be seen. The second objection (shubhat al-inṭibā‘) argues that vision requires an image to be impressed in the eye, but Allah has no form. The third objection (shubhat al-lawn wa al-shakl) states that all visible things have color and shape, established by induction, while Allah is beyond these. The passage concludes: 'This is the totality of their objections in negating the vision.'

Key Terms

المقابلة
الانطباع
اللون
الشكل
العلم الضروري
الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila only argued that seeing Allah is impossible because He has no direction.
Correction: In fact, they had three distinct rational arguments: the argument from facing, the argument from impression, and the argument from color and shape.

Checkpoint

B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila presented three interconnected rational objections—based on facing, impression, and physical attributes—to argue that seeing Allah is impossible.

Refutation of the Dualists
Razi refutes dualists with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the dualist claim that good comes only from light and evil only from darkness using both a metaphysical and a dialectical argument.

Core Idea
Dualism self-destructs when we ask who says 'I erred'.

Imam Razi here engages the dualist sect (al-Thanawiyya) who split creation into two eternal principles: light (source of good) and darkness (source of evil). He first presents a simple metaphysical argument, then shares a clever dialectical refutation from the early masters. The passage shows how a simple action like admitting error can dismantle a whole worldview.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage contains two distinct arguments.
2
Understand the first argument
Razi argues that light is a quality of body, and bodies are proven to be originated, so light must be originated. This undermines the dualist claim that light is eternal.
This argument depends on the earlier proof of the origin of bodies.
3
Follow the classical argument
The early masters use a dialogue: a person says 'I have erred.' Who is speaking? If light, then light produces evil (error or lying). If darkness, then darkness produces good (truth) or evil (lying). Thus both sources produce both good and evil.
This argument is a case analysis with four possibilities.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Notice that regardless of truth or falsehood, the dualist thesis is contradicted. This shows the power of dialectical reasoning in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرقة الثانية من المخالفين في هذه المسألة: الثنوية الذين ينسبون الخير إلى النور والشر إلى الظلمة واعلم أن النور كيفية قائمة بالجسم ونحن قد دللنا على أن الجسم محدث، فكان القول بحدوث النور الذي هو كيفية قائمة بالجسم أولى. وقدماء المشايخ أبطلوا مذهبهم بأن من قال أخطأت فقائل هذا القول إما أن يكون هو النور أو الظلمة. فإن كان هو النور ، فإن كان صدقا فالنور قد فعل الشر، وإن كان كذبا - والكذب شر - فالنور قد فعل الشر وإن كان قائل هذا القول هو - فالظلمة فعلت الخير، وإن كان كذبا الظلمة، فإن كان صدقا - والصدق خير فالظلمة ما فعلت الخطأ. وترك الخطأ خير، فالظلمة فعلت الخير.
And the second group of opponents in this issue: the dualists (al-thanawiyya) who attribute good to light and evil to darkness. Know that light is a quality subsisting in a body, and we have demonstrated that the body is originated, so it is more appropriate to hold that light, which is a quality subsisting in a body, is originated. And the earlier masters (mashayikh) invalidated their doctrine by saying: whoever says 'I have erred' — the speaker of this statement is either light or darkness. If it is light, and he is truthful, then light has done evil; and if he is lying — and lying is evil — then light has done evil. And if the speaker is darkness, and he is truthful — and truth is good — then darkness has done good; and if he is lying — and lying is evil — then darkness has done evil. And refraining from error is good, so darkness has done good.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage refutes the dualists (al-thanawiyya), who claim that good originates from light and evil from darkness. Razi first gives an independent argument: light is a quality of body, and bodies are proven originated, so light is also originated. Then he presents the classical refutation by the early masters: consider a person who says 'I have erred.' That speaker must be either light or darkness. If the speaker is light, then whether the statement is true (he indeed erred, which is evil) or false (lying, also evil), light has produced evil. If the speaker is darkness, then if true (he indeed erred, evil comes from darkness, but truth is good), so darkness has done good (by telling truth), or if false (lying, evil), then darkness has done evil. In either case, the dualist's claim that light only does good and darkness only does evil is contradicted. The OCR text seems to have a slight corruption but the overall argument is preserved. The last sentences stress that refraining from error is good, so darkness avoids evil, implying it does good.

Key Terms
الثنوية
النور
الظلمة
الخير
الشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualism is logically coherent because good and evil come from separate sources.
Correction: Razi shows that the admission of error forces the dualist to admit that both light and darkness produce both good and evil, which contradicts their core claim.

Checkpoint
How do the early masters refute the dualist claim that good comes only from light and evil only from darkness?
A) By arguing that light is a quality of body and body is originated.
B) By pointing out that if a person says 'I erred,' the speaker (light or darkness) is forced to produce both good and evil.
C) By quoting scripture.
D) By proving that darkness does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The dualist division of good and evil into two separate sources fails because any being that speaks must be capable of producing both good and evil, as the act of admitting error shows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a mistake and say 'I erred,' do you attribute that error to a force outside yourself (like shaytan or circumstance) or do you own it as your own action? How does this lesson's demonstration that a single source must produce both good and evil challenge you to take full responsibility for your choices?
?
If you truly believe that only Allah creates both good and evil, why do you still find yourself excusing your sins by blaming others or your environment? What would it look like to live this conviction in your next moment of weakness?
?
The dualists wanted to separate good from evil, but Razi shows they cannot. Where in your life have you tried to compartmentalize your own good deeds and bad deeds, as if they come from different 'selves'? Are you ready to see yourself as a single, accountable servant before Allah?

Refutation of Al-Ka'bi's Claim That God Cannot Create Like Human Acts
Razi refutes al-Ka'bi's argument that God cannot create acts like human acts due to moral attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's argument that God cannot create acts like human acts and Razi's rebuttal that God's power extends to all physical movements and stillnesses, irrespective of moral classification.

Core Idea
Moral qualities do not affect God's power over physical acts.

Al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazili theologian, argued that since human acts are either obedience or disobedience—both impossible for God—God cannot create similar acts. Imam Razi responds by reducing acts to their physical components: movement and stillness. This lesson examines the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Ka'bi's claim
Read the first sentence of the passage where al-Ka'bi asserts that God cannot create acts like human acts because they are either obedience or disobedience. Note the reasoning.
2
Analyze Razi's response
Read Razi's counter: the acts are merely movements or stillnesses, and God is capable of all such physical events. Consider why this response refutes the moral argument.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Does this mean God creates sinful acts? Discuss how the response separates physical creation from moral responsibility.
Razi's point focuses on possibility, not endorsement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما (الكعبي) فإنه يقول : إنه تعالى غير قادر على مثل مقدور العبد، لأن فعل العبد إما طاعة أو معصية. وهما محالان على الله تعالى فثبت أنه تعالى غير قادر على مثل فعل العبد.
والجواب : إن طاعة العبد ومعصيته. إما حركة وإما سكون والله تعالى قادر على جميع الحركات والسكنات.
As for al-Ka'bi, he says: God Most High is not capable of the like of the act of the servant (miṯl maqdūr al-ʿabd), because the act of the servant is either obedience or disobedience. Both are impossible for God Most High, so it is established that God Most High is not capable of the like of the act of the servant.
The response: The obedience and disobedience of the servant are either movement (ḥaraka) or stillness (sukūn), and God Most High is capable of all movements and stillnesses.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This short passage presents al-Ka'bi's argument and Razi's response. Al-Ka'bi claims that God cannot create acts that are like human acts (miṯl maqdūr al-ʿabd) because human acts are classified as obedience or disobedience, both of which cannot be attributed to God. Therefore, God cannot create such acts. Razi responds by analyzing the nature of human acts: they are either movement or stillness. Since God is capable of all movements and stillnesses, He can create the same physical acts regardless of their moral classification. The moral quality does not affect the ontological status of the act as a physical event.

Key Terms
الكعبي
مقدور العبد
طاعة
معصية
حركة
سكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moral qualities affect the possibility of divine power.
Correction: Razi clarifies that God's power relates to the physical act itself, not its moral quality.

Checkpoint
How does Razi respond to al-Ka'bi's argument?
A) By denying that human acts are obedience or disobedience
B) By stating that God can create all movements and stillnesses
C) By arguing that God does not create human acts
D) By claiming that acts are not created by God

Key Takeaway
God's power encompasses all physical movements and stillnesses, irrespective of their moral classification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever think that your sin lies beyond God's power, as if He cannot have created its physical reality? How does Razi's reminder that God creates every movement and stillness challenge your sense of accountability?
?
You often label your actions as 'good' or 'bad' based on their moral outcome. But if moral qualities are accidental, what does that mean for how you assess your intentions? What is the substance of your deeds that you must examine beyond their labels?
?
Razi separates the physical act from its moral attribution. Is there an area of your life where you hide behind the 'neutrality' of an action, convincing yourself that the moral label doesn't matter? How would your behavior change if you fully accepted that God knows every movement and stillness you make, and that He holds you responsible for the moral quality you choose to attach?

Refutation of the Basrian Argument Against One Act from Two Agents
Razi refutes the Basrian claim that one act cannot be within the power of two agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Basrian argument against one act being within the power of two agents and Razi's refutation based on the possibility of multiple causal contributions.

Core Idea
One act can have two agents if one's intention overrides the other's repeller.

In this passage, Razi engages with the Basrian Mu'tazilites who deny that God can create the exact same act as a human. They argue that if two agents could share one act, contradictory outcomes would follow. Razi responds by pointing out a flaw in their reasoning and uses the Mu'tazilite concept of generation (tawallud) to show that multiple agents can indeed contribute to a single act.

Guided Path
1
Read the Basrian argument
Read the Basrian claim and their proof carefully. Note how they use the concepts of motive (dā'ī) and repeller (ṣārif) to argue that one act cannot be within the power of two agents.
The Basrians concede God can create similar acts but not the identical act.
2
Identify the logical contradiction
The Basrians claim that if one act were possible for two agents, conflicting motives would force the act to both exist and not exist. Understand why they think this is impossible.
This is the core of their proof.
3
Examine Razi's first response
Razi questions the premise that the repeller necessarily prevents existence. He suggests that the other agent's intention to create the act can be the cause of its existence, overriding the repeller.
This response invalidates the necessity of the contradiction.
4
Consider the generation (tawallud) example
Razi gives an example from Mu'tazilite thought: if a man leans on a body and movement occurs, the movement is attributed to both the leaner and the body through generation. This shows that multiple agents can be involved in a single act.
The passage is cut off but the point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما البصريون فقد سلموا أنه تعالى قادر على مثل مقدور العبد، إلا أنهم قالوا : إنه تعالى غير قادر على نفس مقدور العبد. واحتجوا عليه بأن ما كان مقدورا للقادر، فلا بد أن يحصل عندما يدعوه الداعي إلى فعله وأن لا يحصل عندما يصرفه الصارف عن فعله فلو فرضنا مقدورا واحدا بين قادرين وحصل الداعي إلى الفعل في حق أحدهما، وحصل الصارف عن الفعل في حق الثاني لزم أن يوجد ذلك الفعل وأن لا يوجد وهذا محال . فالقول بوجود مقدور بین قادرین محال.
والجواب : أن من جوز وجود مقدور واحد لقادرين لم يسلم أنه يلزم من تحقيق الصارف في حق أحد القادرين عن الفعل أن لا يوجد الفعل، لاحتمال أن يكون قصد غيره إلى إيجاده سببا لوجوده.
وأيضا : الرجل إذا اعتمد على جسم آخر، فحدثت حركة في ذلك الجسم الآخر، فقد اتفقت المعتزلة على أن الحركة الحاصلة في ذلك الجسم المباين، إنما حصلت بتأثير وهذه هي المسألة المشهورة بمسألة التولد بالاعتماد وتأثير هذه
As for the Basrians, they conceded that God Most High is capable of the like of the act of the servant, but they said: God Most High is not capable of the very act of the servant (nafs maqdūr al-ʿabd). They argued that whatever is an object of power for someone capable does not fail to occur when the motive (al-dā'ī) calls him to do it, and does not fail to not occur when the repeller (al-ṣārif) turns him away from it. So if we suppose one object of power between two capable agents, and the motive to do the act occurs on the part of one, and the repeller from the act occurs on the part of the second, it would necessitate that the act both exists and does not exist, and this is impossible. Therefore, the statement that there exists an object of power between two capable agents is impossible.
The response: The one who permits one object of power for two capable agents does not concede that the existence of the repeller on the part of one of the two capable agents necessitates that the act does not exist, because it is possible that the intention of another to bring it into existence is a cause of its existence.
Also: If a man leans on another body, and a movement occurs in that other body, the Mu'tazila have agreed that the movement occurring in that distinct body came about through effect (ta'thīr), and this is the famous issue of the issue of generation (al-tawallud) by leaning, and the effect of this...
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents the Basrian argument that God cannot create the very same act as the servant (nafs maqdūr al-ʿabd). They concede God can create similar acts but not the identical act. Their proof: if one act were within the power of two agents, and one agent has a motive to do it while the other has a repeller, the act would both exist (due to the first) and not exist (due to the second), which is contradictory. Therefore, one act cannot be within the power of two agents. Razi responds by questioning the premise: the one who allows one act for two agents does not accept that the repeller necessarily prevents existence, because the other's intention to create it might be the cause of its existence. He then gives an example: if a man leans on a body and movement occurs in it, the Mu'tazila themselves agree that movement is caused by effect (tawallud). This suggests that one act can be brought about by two agents (the leaner and the body?). The passage is cut off but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
البصريون
مقدور واحد
قادرين
الداعي
الصارف
التولد
الاعتماد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is impossible for one act to be within the power of two agents, because that would lead to a logical contradiction.
Correction: Razi shows that the contradiction only follows if the repeller necessarily prevents existence, but that premise is not granted. Moreover, examples like generation (tawallud) illustrate that multiple agents can contribute to a single act.

Checkpoint
What is the Basrian argument against one act being within the power of two agents?
A) It would require two motives
B) It would lead to both existence and non-existence simultaneously
C) God's power is too great
D) Acts are indivisible

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the Basrian argument by showing that the repeller's effect is not absolute and that multiple agents can be involved in a single act through causal chains like generation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you often see your own repeller (your desire) as the sole cause? According to Razi, God's intention can override your repeller. How does this challenge your view of personal responsibility and God's involvement in your actions? Will you now seek God's help to overcome your repellers more earnestly?
?
Think of a good deed you performed recently. Did you attribute it entirely to your own motive (dā'ī)? Razi argues that the same act can be from two agents. How does this affect your gratitude and humility? Will you consciously acknowledge God's role in your righteous actions going forward?
?
The Basrian argument seems logical: if I have a repeller against an act, the act should not happen. But Razi shows that another's intention can override it. In your life, are there acts you felt strongly repelled from but that God intended for you? How can you trust that God's intention, not your repeller, ultimately determines what occurs, and how does this shape your reliance on Him?

The Fourth Opinion: Simple Body as One but Divisible
Razi concludes his survey of opinions on body composition with the fourth opinion and a summary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth opinion on body composition, which holds that the simple body is one in itself but divisible, and how it differs from atomism and the philosophers' view.

Core Idea
The simple body is one but can be divided — a middle position.

In this final segment of his survey, Razi presents the fourth and last opinion on the composition of bodies. Before stating it, he briefly reiterates the philosophers' view that all creation is finite yet God's power is unlimited. The fourth opinion itself offers a nuanced view: the simple body is one in its essence but is capable of division. This lesson unpacks that opinion and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalysing. Then read the translation. Let the flow of Razi's argument come through.
The phrase 'جمهور متناهية' is ambiguous; note it but do not get stuck.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage contains the philosophers' view (first sentence) and the fourth opinion (starting with 'الرابع'). Notice the summary at the end.
3
Focus on the fourth opinion
What does it mean for the simple body to be 'one in itself but divisible'? Consider how this differs from atomism, which posits indivisible parts.
The text says 'قابل لانقسامات' — capable of divisions, not actually divided.
4
Connect to the larger debate
Think about why this opinion matters for theology: it avoids both infinite divisibility and indivisible atoms, offering a middle path.
This connects to discussions about God's creation and the nature of matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل بمعنى أنه تعالى لا يصل في الخلق والإيجاد إلى حد، إلا ويمكنه بعد يوجد شيئا آخر وإن كل ما يخرج إلى الوجود فإنه متناه. وهذا مذهب الفلاسفة. جمهور متناهية. الرابع : قول من يقول : الجسم البسيط واحد في نفسه، لكنه قابل لانقسامات فهذا هو تفصيل المذاهب في هذا الباب.
Rather meaning that He, exalted, does not reach a limit in creation and bringing into existence, except that after it He can bring into existence another thing, and that everything that comes into existence is finite. This is the doctrine of the philosophers. [Some] multitude [say?] finite. Fourth: the saying of one who says: the simple body is one in itself, but is divisible [in potential]. So this is the detailed account of the schools in this chapter.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents the final part of a classification of positions on the composition of bodies. It begins by stating the view of the philosophers: that God's creation does not reach a limit such that He cannot create something further, and that everything that comes into existence is finite. Then it mentions the fourth opinion, which holds that the simple body is one in itself but is capable of division. The passage concludes by stating that this is the full enumeration of the schools on this topic. The structure is enumerative: the preceding opinions (not shown) are implied, and this is the final one with a summary.

Key Terms

تفصيل المذاهب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth opinion is that the body is composed of indivisible atoms (atomism).
Correction: The text says the simple body is one but divisible [in potential], not composed of indivisible parts. Atomism holds actual indivisible parts.

Checkpoint

B) It is one in itself but divisible [in potential].
C) It is infinitely divisible.
D) It is non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The fourth opinion offers a middle path: the simple body is one in essence but potentially divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's creative power has no limit, how does that challenge the way you think about the size of your own problems? Are you living as if His power is enough for what seems impossible to you?
?
The philosophers held that everything that comes into existence is finite. How does that truth affect the way you prioritize your time, energy, and attachments? What would change if you truly lived with the certainty that nothing here is eternal?
?
The fourth opinion says the simple body is one but potentially divisible. Reflect on the times you let yourself be divided in your devotion to Allah—by distractions, worries, or divided loyalties. What would it take to gather yourself back into unity of purpose?

The Philosophers' Argument for the Soul's Indestructibility and Its Refutation
Razi presents and refutes the philosophers' argument on the soul's indestructibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' first argument for the eternity of human souls and how Imam Razi refutes it by showing the impossibility of intrinsic or extrinsic susceptibility to non-existence.

Core Idea
The soul cannot become non-existent because its annihilation would need to coexist with its existence—an impossibility.

This passage presents the first and main philosophical argument for the eternity of human souls, followed by a rigorous refutation. Imam Razi examines the claim that souls are not susceptible to annihilation, systematically dismantling the logical premises. By working through this argument, you will learn a key dialectical method in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation once to get the flow. Notice the structure: first the argument, then the objection and response.
2
Identify the two divisions
The argument divides susceptibility to non-existence into two possibilities: intrinsic or extrinsic. Follow how the author proves both are impossible.
This is a classic 'division and refute' method.
3
Examine the objection and answer
The objection claims this reasoning would apply to all forms and accidents. See how the author distinguishes the soul from material forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة فقد احتجوا على قولهم بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : - وهي عمدتهم - أن الأرواح البشرية لا تقبل الفناء، وتقريرها أنها لو كانت قابلة للعدم لكانت قابليتها للعدم إما أن تكون قائمة بها أو بغيرها والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بكونها قابلة للعدم. وإنما قلنا بأنه يمتنع أن تكون هذه القابلية قائمة بها ؛ لأن كل ما كان قابلا بشيء فإن القابل يكون متقررًا مع المقبول، فلو كان الموصوف بقابلية عدمها، نفس وجودها لزم أن يتقرر وجودها مع عدمها، وذلك محال، وإنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع أن تكون هذه القابلية قائمة بغيرها؛ لأن مثل هذا الشيء يجب أن يكون صورة في مادة حتى يكون إمكان وجودها وإمكان عدمها قائما بتلك المادة فإن النفس إن كانت كذلك مركبة من مادة وصورة لكن النفس جوهر مجرد فمادتها يجب أن تكون جوهرا مجردًا، وحينئذ نقول : إن كانت تلك المادة أيضًا قابلة للعدم افتقرت إلى مادة أخرى، ولزم التسلسل، وهو محال. فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى مادة أخيرة لا مادة لها وتلك المادة لا تكون قابلة للعدم - وهي جوهر مجرد - فهي محل العلوم والإدراكات، فتكون النفس هي غير فتكون النفس باقية غير قابلة للعدم. فإن قيل : فيلزمكم أن لا يكون شيء من الصور والأعراض قابلا للعدم. قلنا : هذا غير لازم؛ لأن القابل لصحة حدوثها ولصحة عدمها موادها، وموادها متقررة مع ذلك الحدوث وذلك الحدوث وذلك العدم، بخلاف جوهر النفس، فإنها إن كانت بريئة عن المادة فقد ظهر الفرق، وإن كانت مادتها لا محالة جوهرًا مجردًا فتكون النفس هي هي لا غير، فتكون باقية.
As for the philosophers, they have argued for their view with proofs: The first proof - which is their mainstay - is that human souls do not admit of annihilation. Its presentation: If they were susceptible to non-existence, their susceptibility to non-existence would either be inherent in them or in something else, and both divisions are false, so the statement that they are susceptible to non-existence is false. We say that it is impossible for this susceptibility to be inherent in them because everything that is susceptible by something, the susceptor must coexist with the received. So if that which is described by the susceptibility of its own non-existence were its own existence, it would require that its existence coexist with its non-existence, and that is impossible. We say that it is impossible for this susceptibility to be in something else because such a thing must be a form in a matter so that its possibility of existence and its possibility of non-existence are in that matter. For if the soul were thus composed of matter and form, but the soul is an immaterial substance, so its matter must be an immaterial substance. Then we say: If that matter is also susceptible to non-existence, it would need another matter, leading to infinite regress, which is impossible. So one must end at a final matter that has no matter, and that matter is not susceptible to non-existence - and it is an immaterial substance - so it is the locus of sciences and perceptions, thus the soul is that [matter] and thus the soul remains not susceptible to non-existence. If it is said: This would entail that no form or accident is susceptible to non-existence. We say: This is not necessary because the susceptor for the possibility of their occurrence and the possibility of their non-existence is their matters, and their matters coexist with that occurrence and that non-existence, unlike the essence of the soul, for if it is free from matter, the difference has become apparent; and if its matter is necessarily an immaterial substance, then the soul is [that matter] itself and nothing else, so it remains.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' first argument for the eternity of human souls and the author's refutation. The argument claims that if souls were susceptible to non-existence, that susceptibility would either be intrinsic (in the soul itself) or extrinsic (in something else). Both options are impossible: intrinsic susceptibility would require the soul's existence to coexist with its non-existence; extrinsic susceptibility would require the soul to be a form in a matter, but the soul is an immaterial substance, so its matter would also be immaterial and would itself need another matter, leading to infinite regress. The author then responds to a potential objection: if this reasoning is applied to all forms and accidents, they too would be indestructible. The answer is that forms and accidents are not immaterial; their matters are the susceptors and coexist with them, unlike the soul which has no matter or whose matter is its own immaterial self.

Key Terms
الأرواح البشرية
الفناء
القابلية
الجوهر المجرد
المادة
الصورة
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' argument convincingly proves the soul cannot be annihilated.
Correction: Razi shows the argument fails because it either leads to a contradiction (intrinsic susceptibility) or infinite regress (extrinsic susceptibility) unless the soul is identified with its immaterial substrate, which then makes it not a soul in the usual sense.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the philosophers' claim that the susceptibility to non-existence cannot be intrinsic to the soul?
A) It would require the soul's existence to coexist with its non-existence
B) The soul is immaterial
C) It leads to infinite regress
D) The soul is eternal by nature

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' first argument for the soul's eternity fails because it cannot coherently explain where the susceptibility to non-existence would reside without contradiction or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed your soul is indestructible and not susceptible to annihilation, how would that change your daily choices about what you prioritize? What is one thing today you would do differently based on that belief?
?
The argument hinges on the soul being immaterial and not dependent on matter. Do you live as if your ultimate reality is your physical existence or your soul? What evidence in your daily habits supports your answer?
?
Razi shows that the philosophers' argument fails to prove the soul's indestructibility. Does this make you more or less certain of the soul's immortality? Why? And if you are uncertain, what concrete steps will you take to seek certainty about what happens after death?

The Philosophers' Second Argument: Time Without Beginning or End
al-Razi presents the philosophers' second argument, enumerating three premises and beginning the proof of the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first premise of the philosophers' second argument for the eternity of the world: the claim that time has no beginning and the reasoning based on the concept of 'precedence' (qabliyya).

Core Idea
The philosophers argue that time cannot begin because the 'before' would itself be time.

Here, al-Razi presents the second argument of the philosophers for the eternity of the world. The argument rests on three premises, the first being that time has no beginning and no end. In this passage, we see how they attempt to prove that time cannot have a beginning. Pay attention to the key concept of 'precedence' (qabliyya) and why it leads to a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three premises
Read the opening sentence to see the three premises of the argument: time has no beginning/end, motion is eternal, body is eternal.
These are the building blocks of the philosophers' case.
2
Focus on the first premise
The passage explains only the first premise: time has no beginning. Find the argument for beginninglessness.
The proof for no end is cut off.
3
Understand the role of 'precedence'
The key idea: if time began, non-existence preceded it. But that precedence (qabliyya) is not the same as non-existence itself; it is an existing attribute. This implies a time before time, a contradiction.
Non-existence is uniform; 'before' and 'after' are not distinctions within non-existence.
4
Note the incompleteness
The passage ends mid-sentence. The full argument and the author's refutation come later. This is only the presentation of the philosophers' view.
Do not assume al-Razi accepts this argument.
5
Reflect on the argument's structure
Trace how the philosophers use time's eternity to prove motion's eternity, then the body's eternity. This is a chain of reasoning.
The first premise is the most fundamental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية لهم : الابتداء والانتهاء على الزمان محال، فوجب أن تكون هي الحركة كذلك، فوجب أن يكون الجسم كذلك، فهذه مقدمات ثلاثة. المقدمة الأولى : قولنا : لا بداية للزمان ولا نهاية له، فنقول : أما قولنا يمتنع أن تكون للزمان بداية فذلك لأن كل ما لوجوده بداية فعدمه متقدم على وجوده، وذلك التقدم ليس نفس العدم ؛ لأن العدم قبل كالعدم بعد وليس القبل بعد، فالقبلية ليست نفس العدم، فهي صفة موجودة، فقد حصل قبل مبدأ الزمان شيء آخر متقدم عليه بالزمان، هذا خلف. فإذن لا بداية للزمان وأما أنه لا نهاية له فلأنا لو فرضنا له نهاية لكانت تلك النهاية
The second proof for them: The beginning and end of time are impossible, so motion must be [eternal] as well, so the body must be [eternal] as well. These are three premises. The first premise: Our statement: Time has no beginning and no end. We say: As for our statement that it is impossible for time to have a beginning, that is because everything whose existence has a beginning, its non-existence precedes its existence, and that precedence is not itself non-existence, because non-existence before is like non-existence after, and the 'before' is not 'after', so 'beforeness' is not itself non-existence; it is an existing attribute. Thus something else prior to the beginning of time would have occurred, preceding it in time. This is a contradiction. Therefore, time has no beginning. As for its having no end, because if we suppose for it an end, that end would be
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage begins the presentation of the philosophers' second argument for the eternity of the world. The argument has three premises: (1) Time has no beginning and no end, (2) from which it follows that motion is eternal, and (3) that body is eternal. The author then commences explaining the first premise: if time had a beginning, then non-existence would precede its existence, and that precedence (قبلية) would not be non-existence itself because non-existence does not involve temporal priority. That precedence would be an attribute existing before the beginning of time, which would be a prior time, leading to a contradiction. Therefore time has no beginning. The proof for no end begins but is cut off. The passage is incomplete.

Key Terms
الزمان
البداية
النهاية
الحركة
الجسم
القبلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' premise that time has no beginning is accepted by the author.
Correction: This passage only presents the philosophers' argument; al-Razi later refutes it. The author does not endorse this view.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' first premise, why is it impossible for time to have a beginning?
A) Because the precedence of non-existence would be an existing attribute before time, leading to a contradiction
B) Because time is a necessary being
C) Because motion is eternal
D) Because the soul is eternal

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that time cannot begin because the precedence of non-existence would itself be a temporal attribute, leading to an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time had no beginning, then you have always been in the sight of your Creator, even before your own existence. How does this realization affect your sense of accountability for every moment granted to you?
?
The philosophers argue that 'beforeness' is an attribute that exists, not mere non-existence. Is there a 'before' in your life—a sin or a missed obligation—that you have dismissed as 'nothing' but which still has a real attribute that God sees?
?
If the world is eternal, as these philosophers claim, then your entire life is infinitesimal in an endless timeline. Does that make you feel insignificant, or does it press you to ask: 'Why has God given me this particular moment to know Him?'
