The Obligation of Imamate: Revelation vs. Reason
Imam Razi classifies opinions on the obligation of imamate, focusing on those who say it is obligatory and the first subdivision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main perspectives on the obligation of imamate: those who base it on revelation and those who base it on reason, and the first subgroup that places the obligation on the people.

Core Idea
The obligation of imamate is not agreed upon; some ground it in revelation, others in reason.

Imam al-Razi begins his discussion of imamate by classifying the different opinions on whether appointing an imam is obligatory. He first categorizes the views into those who say it is obligatory and those who say it is not, then focuses on the obligatory view. This lesson covers the initial classification up to the view that the obligation is upon the people themselves by reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the classification system Imam Razi uses.
Notice the logical structure: first a division, then a subdivision.
2
Identify the two main groups
Within those who say obligatory, find the two groups: those who rely on revelation and those who rely on reason. Note their examples.
The Ash'aris, most Mu'tazila, and Zaydiyya are in the revelation group.
3
Examine the 'reason on the people' view
Understand why some Mu'tazila argue that reason obligates the people to appoint a leader to repel harm.
This view is attributed to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and earlier figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة والثلاثون
في الإمامة
وفيها فصول :
الفصل الأول : في وجوب الإمامة
اختلف الناس فيه، وضبط المذاهب فيه أن يقال : نصب الإمام إما أن يقال: واجب أو غير واجب، أما القائلون بأنه واجب فهم فريقان :
أحدهما : الذين قالوا : نصبه واجب والطريق إلى معرفة هذا الوجوب السمع دون العقل، وهذا هو قول أصحابنا وأكثر المعتزلة والزيدية.
والثاني : الذين يقولون: الطريق إلى معرفة هذا الوجوب العقل، ثم هؤلاء فريقان، منهم من قال إنه يجب عقلا على الخلق أن ينصبوا لأنفسهم رئيساً، وذلك لأن نصب هذا الرئيس يتضمن دفع الضرر عن النفس ودفع الضرر عن النفس واجب عقلا. وهذا هو قول أبي الحسين البصري من المعتزلة ومن قدمائهم قول الجاحظ وأبي الحسين الخياط وأبي القاسم الكعبي.
ومنهم من قال : بل يجب على الله نصب الإمام للخلق، وهؤلاء فريقان: قالت الملاحدة والإسماعيلية : إنه لا سبيل إلى معرفة الله إلا بتعليم الرسول والإمام، فوجب على الله تعالى أن لا يخلي العالم عن المعصوم حتى إن ذلك المعصوم يرشد الخلق إلى معرفة الله تعالى .
وقالت الإثنا عشرية : لا حاجة في معرفة الله تعالى إلى المعصوم إلا أنه يجب على الله تعالى نصب الإمام المعصوم ليكون لطفا في أداء الواجبات العقلية، والاجتناب عن القبائح العقلية، وليكون أيضًا حافظا للدين عن الزيادة والنقصان. وقال بعض قدماء الشيعة إنه يجب على الله نصب الإمام ليعلمهم أحوال
The Thirty-Ninth Issue
On Imamate
And it contains chapters:
The First Chapter: On the Obligation of Imamate
People differed regarding it, and the summary of the schools regarding it is that the appointment of an imam is either said to be obligatory or not obligatory. As for those who say it is obligatory, they are two groups:
One of them: Those who said: His appointment is obligatory, and the way to know this obligation is revelation without reason. This is the view of our companions (Ash'aris) and most of the Mu'tazila and the Zaydiyya.
The second: Those who say: The way to know this obligation is reason. Then these are two groups: among them are those who said that it is obligatory by reason upon the people to appoint for themselves a leader, because the appointment of this leader entails repelling harm from oneself, and repelling harm from oneself is obligatory by reason. This is the view of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri of the Mu'tazila, and among their early ones the view of al-Jahiz, Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat, and Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi.
And among them are those who said: Rather, it is obligatory upon God to appoint an imam for the people. These are two groups: The Mulahida and the Isma'iliyya said: There is no way to know God except through the teaching of the Messenger and the imam, so it is obligatory upon God Most High not to leave the world without an infallible one, so that that infallible one guides the people to knowledge of God Most High.
And the Ithna 'Ashariyya said: There is no need in knowing God Most High for the infallible one, but it is obligatory upon God Most High to appoint the infallible imam to be a grace (lutf) in performing the rational duties and refraining from rational abominations, and also to be a protector of the religion from increase and decrease. And some early Shi'a said that it is obligatory upon God to appoint the imam to teach them the conditions...
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a classification of opinions on the obligation of imamate. Imam al-Razi begins by stating that people differ, and he frames the classification around whether the appointment of an imam is obligatory or not. He then focuses on those who say it is obligatory. They divide into two: one group says the obligation is known by revelation (the Ash'aris, most Mu'tazila, and Zaydiyya). The other group says it is known by reason, and they subdivide: some say reason imposes the obligation upon the people themselves to appoint a leader (citing repelling harm as a rational duty). This view is attributed to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and earlier Mu'tazila like al-Jahiz, al-Khayyat, and al-Ka'bi. Others say the obligation is upon God to appoint an imam. These are further divided: the Mulahida (here meaning Batinis/Ismailis) and Ismailis hold that knowledge of God requires an infallible teacher, so God must provide one. The Twelvers say there is no need for an infallible in knowing God, but God must appoint one as a grace (lutf) to assist in performing rational duties and to protect the religion from alteration. Finally, some early Shi'a held that God must appoint an imam to teach people the conditions (of religion). The text cuts off here, but the classification is complete as far as the available text goes.

Key Terms
إمامة
نصب الإمام
وجوب
سمع
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all Muslims agree that the imam must be appointed by divine command.
Correction: This passage shows that there is disagreement on the source of obligation (revelation vs reason) and on whom the obligation falls (people vs God).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group holds that the obligation of imamate is known by revelation?
A) Twelver Shi'a
B) Ash'aris, most Mu'tazila, and Zaydiyya
C) Ismailis
D) Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and his followers

Key Takeaway
The obligation of imamate is a contested issue, with some basing it on revelation and others on reason, and even among the rationalists, there are different views on who bears the obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even the 'obligation' of imamate is debated among scholars. When you hold a religious opinion, do you assume it is the only valid one? How can you guard against dismissing contrary views without understanding their evidence? Commit today to read one primary source from a differing position on a contested issue.
?
The rationalists argue that repelling harm (by appointing a leader) is a self-evident duty. Reflect: have you ever avoided participating in community affairs because it was inconvenient, while harm was being done? What step will you take this week to actively contribute to the well-being of your community?
?
The Ash'aris ground the obligation in revelation. Do your political and communal decisions truly follow revelation, or do you prioritize your own reasoning and desires? How can you realign your loyalties so that the Qur'an and Sunnah are your ultimate guides, even when they challenge your preferences?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
5 min
The Obligation upon God to Appoint an Imam
Imam Razi classifies the views that impose the obligation of imamate on God, including Ismaili, Twelver, and early Shi'a opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opinions of those who hold that the obligation to appoint an imam falls upon God, including the Ismaili, Twelver, and early Shi'a views.

Core Idea
Some groups argue that God must provide an infallible imam for guidance or as a grace.

After discussing those who place the obligation on the people, Imam al-Razi turns to those who say the obligation is upon God. He further divides this camp into the Ismailis, who see the infallible imam as necessary for knowing God, the Twelvers, who see him as a grace (lutf) for performing duties and preserving the religion, and some early Shi'a who hold a similar view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for this section, noting the three subgroups mentioned.
The text cuts off at the end, but the main views are clear.
2
Compare the Ismaili and Twelver perspectives
Notice the difference: Ismailis say the infallible is required for knowing God, while Twelvers say he is not needed for that but is a grace.
Both agree on the obligation upon God to appoint an imam.
3
Reflect on the early Shi'a view
The text mentions that some early Shi'a held that God must appoint an imam to teach people. Consider how this differs from the other views.
The passage ends abruptly, but the idea is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة والثلاثون
في الإمامة
وفيها فصول :
الفصل الأول : في وجوب الإمامة
اختلف الناس فيه، وضبط المذاهب فيه أن يقال : نصب الإمام إما أن يقال: واجب أو غير واجب، أما القائلون بأنه واجب فهم فريقان :
أحدهما : الذين قالوا : نصبه واجب والطريق إلى معرفة هذا الوجوب السمع دون العقل، وهذا هو قول أصحابنا وأكثر المعتزلة والزيدية.

The Thirty-Ninth Issue
On Imamate
And it contains chapters:
The First Chapter: On the Obligation of Imamate
People differed regarding it, and the summary of the schools regarding it is that the appointment of an imam is either said to be obligatory or not obligatory. As for those who say it is obligatory, they are two groups:
One of them: Those who said: His appointment is obligatory, and the way to know this obligation is revelation without reason. This is the view of our companions (Ash'aris) and most of the Mu'tazila and the Zaydiyya.
The second: Those who say: The way to know this obligation is reason. Then these are two groups: among them are those who said that it is obligatory by reason upon the people to appoint for themselves a leader, because the appointment of this leader entails repelling harm from oneself, and repelling harm from oneself is obligatory by reason. This is the view of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri of the Mu'tazila, and among their early ones the view of al-Jahiz, Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat, and Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi.
And among them are those who said: Rather, it is obligatory upon God to appoint an imam for the people. These are two groups: The Mulahida and the Isma'iliyya said: There is no way to know God except through the teaching of the Messenger and the imam, so it is obligatory upon God Most High not to leave the world without an infallible one, so that that infallible one guides the people to knowledge of God Most High.

And some early Shi'a said that it is obligatory upon God to appoint the imam to teach them the conditions...

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all Shi'a agree on the necessity of an infallible imam for knowledge of God.
Correction: The passage shows disagreement: Ismailis affirm this necessity, but Twelvers do not; they see the imam as a grace, not a requirement for knowing God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Twelver view on the appointment of an infallible imam?
A) It is necessary for knowing God
B) It is a grace (lutf) for performing duties and preserving religion
C) It is not obligatory
D) It is obligatory only if the people request it

Key Takeaway
Among those who say the obligation is on God, there are differences on why God must appoint an infallible imam: for necessary guidance (Ismaili), as a gracious aid (Twelver), or for teaching (early Shi'a).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Isma'ilis claim you cannot know God without an infallible teacher. Do you rely on any human to know God, or do you trust that God has made Himself known sufficiently through revelation and reason? Examine your own sources of certainty: are they truly independent of any fallible human authority?
?
The Twelvers say an infallible imam is not needed to know God, but is a grace to help you perform duties. If you had an infallible guide today, would your obedience increase? Or does the absence of such a guide expose a weakness in your own resolve to follow God's commands without external enforcement?
?
Early Shi'a said the imam is to teach people. Whom do you turn to for religious teaching? How does your choice reflect your belief – or lack thereof – that God has provided adequate guidance through the Qur'an and the Prophet's example alone?
